==PAGE:1==
Analysing and Evaluating the QLattice as an
Evolutionary Algorithm in the Symbolic Regression Space
Emil Lundt Larsen (s153214)
Supervisor (DTU): Carsten Witt
Supervisors (Abzu): Meera Machado, Valdemar Stentoft-Hansen
June 11, 2021
==PAGE:2==
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science
Richard Petersens Plads, building 324,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Phone +45 4525 3031
compute@compute.dtu.dk
www.compute.dtu.dk
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The purpose of this study is to analyse and evaluate the QLattice algorithm de-
veloped by Abzu ApS using theory and methods from evolutionary algorithms and
compare it with genetic programming for symbolic regression. Through a wide range
of benchmarks on regression and classiﬁcation problems based on both synthetic and
real world data sets, a comprehensive analysis of the eﬀect of diﬀerent hyper-parameter
settings and evolutionary techniques applied to the QLattice is carried out. In addition
to this, a custom genetic programming framework and a dashboard web application
have been implemented for visualising key metrics extracted from the run of the QLat-
tice with a chosen conﬁguration and data set.
The results show that the QLattice
was able to produce simple and accurate models without prior assumptions of the
model structure.
It also worked well as an oﬀ-the-shelf algorithm with the default
hyper-parameters; thus, avoiding the need for an extensive grid search for optimal
hyper-parameters on every individual test case. Yet, the non-default Bayesian infor-
mation criterion parameter was found to work the best with QLattice, which lead to
simpler models on a par in predictive performance. An increase in the evolutionary
hyper-parameters n and τ in the formula for the graph decay threshold and a higher
mutation strength also yielded the best results, which suggests that there could be a
beneﬁt in adjusting these default parameters for the algorithm. Finally, a comparison
with the state-of-the-art genetic programming based symbolic regression algorithm,
Eureqa, was made, where the QLattice achieved competitive results.

1
INTRODUCTION
1
Introduction
Regression and classiﬁcation problems are ubiquitous in many branches of engineering and
research and it is of great interest to be able to ﬁnd accurate and interpretable models for
them [19]. A vast number of supervised machine learning algorithms exist in the literature,
but many of them either rely on strong assumptions of the structure of the data or produce
black-box models that can be diﬃcult to interpret.
The QLattice developed by the AI company Abzu [2] aims at addressing both of these is-
sues with a novel symbolic regression algorithm inspired by quantum ﬁeld theory [56]. While
both showing eﬃciency and accuracy, the QLattice produces models that describe the rela-
tionship between input features and the target of a problem as interpretable mathematical
formulas. Yet, the QLattice has many things in common with the classic evolutionary tech-
nique, genetic programming, which is used in for instance the GPLearn [50] and Eureqa [11]
systems.
This motivates the analysis of the properties and strengths and weaknesses of
both approaches as well as an investigation of how well the algorithms compare on diﬀerent
problems and the eﬀect of diﬀerent choices of hyper-parameters. The aim of this project
is to analyse the QLattice as an evolutionary algorithm in the symbolic regression space
using theory and methods from the ﬁeld of evolutionary algorithms, investigate the eﬀect
and applicability of diﬀerent settings of evolutionary hyper-parameters and compare the
performance with genetic programming for symbolic regression.
The report is structured as follows: Initially, an account of the relevant background
theory for the project is given in Section 2. Section 3 presents related work with other
symbolic regression algorithms and benchmarks of symbolic regression algorithms. Following
this we make a preliminary analysis of the QLattice in Section 4 in order to understand how
and to what extent it shares features with evolutionary algorithms. In Section 5 we delimit
the test cases and details of the experiments to carry out and Section 6 contains details of the
implementation of some part of the software developed during the project. In Section 7 we
evaluate the QLattice on diﬀerent types of problems and with diﬀerent parameter settings
and compare with genetic programming. Finally in Section 8 we discuss and reﬂect on some
of the main ﬁndings and suggest possible directions for future work and we conclude the
study in Section 9. References, a list of ﬁgures and tables and experiment results are listed
at the end of the report.
1
==PAGE:10==
2
BACKGROUND
2
Background
In this section we describe some of the background theory relevant for the project. This
includes a description of symbolic regression, genetic programming and the QLattice algo-
rithm. More details on how the QLattice works are given in Section 4.1. We also give a
succinct account of some of the fundamental terms from machine learning in general includ-
ing the notion of expected prediction error, the bias-variance trade-oﬀand the regression
and classiﬁcation performance metrics used later in the evaluation in Section 7. This will
provide a self-contained basis and terminology for the work with the analysis and evaluation.
The primary sources for Section 2.1 and 2.2 are [26, 40, 28] and the main sources for Section
2.4 are [7, 19]. The main sources for Section 2.3 are [41, 56, 1, 3]; some details are also from
discussions with Abzu.
2.1
Symbolic Regression
The aim of symbolic regression (SR) is to ﬁnd a mathematical formula with some property.
Typically the property we seek is to model the structure of a data set D with a set of
predictors x1, x2, . . . and response y as well as possible with respect to some performance
metric [40, 26] (see Section 2.4.1). An important aspect of symbolic regression is that no
model structure is assumed a priori. This is contrary to standard regression analysis, where
the goal is to ﬁnd the coeﬃcients and parameters of a model with a certain structure. For
instance in ordinary least squares (OLS), the model f(x) = a0 + a1x1 + · · · + anxn, where
a0, a1, . . . , an ∈R, has a linear structure and ﬁtting the model amounts to computing the
best coeﬃcients a0, a1 . . . , an based on the data available [7]. A clear advantage of symbolic
regression is therefore that it removes the need for the modeller to spell out the model
structure and can avoid the need to test diﬀerent model structures manually. On the other
hand, a drawback is that the search space can be larger by virtue of ﬁnding the model
structure being an inherent part of the problem [46, 40, 28].
The search space in symbolic regression consists of mathematical formulas meaning the
operators may take the form of addition, subtraction, multiplication, the square root, the sine
function etc. In principle with the right function set, any relationship between the predictor
and response of a data set can be modelled perfectly with enough operators and real weights
[23]. However, we are typically in addition to accuracy interested in interpretable models,
so it is common to restrict or apply methods that limit the number of operations the models
can contain. Intuitively when the formulas are small, the model explaining the data is easier
to interpret, which can be a strength of using symbolic regression compared to some other
approaches such as support vector machines or artiﬁcial neural networks [40]. We discuss
performance and interpretability in more detail in Section 2.4.
A symbolic regression model can be represented in a number of ways. Apart from a
mathematical formula, the formula is sometimes written in Polish preﬁx notation. It is also
common to represent the formula with a syntax tree structure that shows the order in which
the sub-expressions are evaluated. Figure 2.1 shows an example of a symbolic regression
model in the three diﬀerent representations.
2.2
Genetic Programming
Genetic programming (GP) can be deﬁned as a collection of evolutionary computation tech-
niques that allow a computer system to generate solutions to problems automatically [26,
40]. This is done by stochastically transforming a population of programs over a number
of generations using various evolutionary operations. The term program is often used in
genetic programming literature for the individuals of the population even if they do not
directly represent a computer program with executable code.

mathematical formula the model represents. Right (bottom): Formula in polish preﬁx notation. Example
from [50].
What sets genetic programming apart from the more general evolutionary algorithm
class, genetic algorithms (GA), is that the candidate solutions are traditionally represented
with a tree structure, though derivatives such as linear genetic programming exist [40]. The
evolutionary operators used therefore transform the structure of trees (see Section 2.2.1).
The tree structure also makes genetic programming readily applicable for a wide range of
problems including symbolic regression.
The general structure of the genetic programming algorithm is shown as a ﬂowchart in
Figure 2.2. Initially a population of random programs is created followed by the main loop,
where individuals are selected and either mutated, crossed or reproduced to form a new
population for the next generation. Most commonly when creating a new oﬀspring from one
or two parent programs, either crossover or mutation is performed, but not both [26, 40, 50].
Though, performing mutation after crossover is also valid. In this project we limit ourselves
to a select subset of common evolutionary operators for symbolic regression as this is the
focus of the project.
2.2.1
Evolutionary Operators
Initialisation:
The most commonly used ways of initialising the population are grow, full
and (ramped) half-and-half [26]. The full method generates complete random trees of depth
d while the grow method generates trees by randomly selecting functions from the function
set and terminals at random leaf node points until the tree reaches depth d. The half-and-
half method combines grow and full with 50 % of the trees generated using each method,
which should lead to a more diverse structure of trees in the initial population [26]. To
achieve even greater variety in the initial population, it is common to choose an interval
[d1, d2] (typically [2, 6]) of depths to be used such that a fraction
1
d2−d1 of the trees are
generated half-and-half for each d ∈[d1, d2]. Hence, the name ramped half-and-half, which
is the method Koza advises to use in general [26].
Selection:
Koza originally used ﬁtness proportionate selection [26] as selection operator,
where the probability of an individual being chosen equals the fraction that its ﬁtness makes
up of the total sum of the ﬁtness in the population.
Rank selection is similar to ﬁtness proportionate selection but the selection is based on
rank instead of the ﬁtness value itself [40]. The rank of an individual is its index in the
ﬁtness of the individuals in sorted order from best to worst. There are slightly diﬀerent
ways of assigning rank to the individuals, but commonly the best individual would get a
weighting of N, the second best N −1 and so on down to 1, where N is the population size.
Observe that the gap in ﬁtness between two individuals in sorted order is irrelevant in rank
selection contrary to ﬁtness proportionate selection, which can both be advantageous and a

2.2
Genetic Programming
2
BACKGROUND
drawback.
One of the most common selection schemes is tournament selection, where M individuals
are chosen uniformly at random from the population [40]. The best individual among these,
with respect to ﬁtness, is then selected.
The number M of individuals chosen for each
tournament aﬀects the variance in the ﬁtness of the selected individuals. A low value M
will lead to more individuals with relatively low ﬁtness being selected compared to a large
setting of M, which makes the individuals with the best ﬁtness getting selected more often.
The selection schemes above can be combined with the elitist selection scheme, where
at the start of a new generation, the best individual from the previous generation is copied
over into the new population. This has the beneﬁt of ensuring that the best program found
so far always survives, but can bias the search and have adverse eﬀects for small population
sizes [26, 40].
Crossover:
The most commonly used form of crossover in genetic programming is subtree
crossover [40]. Subtree crossover between two parents 1 and 2 happens by randomly choosing
a mutation point in parent 1 and 2 and then replacing the mutation point in parent 1 with
the subtree rooted at the mutation point in parent 2 (see Figure 2.3 for an example). This
can both lead to an oﬀspring with larger size than the parents and a tree with smaller size.
+

Offspring
Figure 2.3: Example of subtree crossover [40].
Another crossover operator is one-point crossover, where a mutation point common to
both parent programs is randomly selected and two oﬀspring are then created by swapping
the subtrees of each parent rooted at the mutation point. The common mutation point is
found by overlaying the trees from the root [26, 40].
Mutation:
Common mutation operators include subtree mutation, point mutation and
hoist mutation [40]. Subtree mutation of a tree T works by randomly choosing a mutation
point in T and then replacing that mutation point with a randomly generated tree (see Figure
2.4 for an example). Note that the subtree mutation operator is the same as applying subtree
crossover on the mutation point with a randomly generated subtree.
Point mutation changes a random node in the tree by a legal substitution of the function
or terminal at the mutation point [40]. This means that an internal node with a function of
arity n would be replaced by another random function of arity n and that a leaf node with
terminal t would be replaced by another random terminal t′ ̸= t.
In hoist mutation a random mutation point in a tree T is chosen. Let T1 be the subtree
rooted at the mutation point chosen. In T1 another mutation point is then chosen randomly
giving a descendent subtree T2. T2 is then ”hoisted” as T1 is replaced with T2 [40].
Reproduction:
Finally, the reproduction operator simply copies an individual from the
population to the new population of the next generation. Reproduction brings some stability
to the evolutionary process, but can stagnate the search if it is used too often [26, 40].

2.2.2
Closure and Suﬃciency
During the search process of genetic programming it is not unlikely that some of the programs
of the population will be exhibiting very extreme output values or be outright invalid for the
problem, which can cause problems when evaluating the programs during the search process
[26, 40, 50]. For example in symbolic regression a program corresponding to the formula
1/x would result in division by zero when the input feature x = 0. In order to mitigate this
it is common practice to apply measures such as redeﬁning the division function as follows:
1/x :=
(
1
if |x| < ε

otherwise

Using the protected versions of the numeric functions provide closure and better numerical
stability, but comes at the expense of some interpretability for some input values. There are
other ways of deﬁning the protected operators, but this is the way Koza [26] suggested it orig-

GPLearn [50]. One noteworthy alternative to the protected division operator as deﬁned
above in equation (2.1) is the analytic quotient operator, which Ni et al.
[35] show can

can result from using either protected or unprotected division. The analytic quotient (AQ)

Another aspect to consider when using genetic programming for symbolic regression is
the suﬃciency in the function set [26, 50].
If the algorithm can only use addition and
multiplication for example, data following a sine wave or exponential function cannot be
modelled accurately by a small sized model. On the other hand, including many numeric
functions in the function set makes the search space larger as there are more possible models
of a given size and it may lead to higher variance, however.

==PAGE:15==
2.2
Genetic Programming
2

2.2.3
Bloat
Bloat refers to redundant substructures in the programs in the population and is an im-
portant aspect in genetic programming, as it can aﬀect both the interpretability and the
predictive performance of the models on out-of-sample (unseen) data [40]. Unusable parts
of the programs/trees are sometimes called introns, which is a term from genetics. When
running a genetic programming algorithm many generations it is common that at some point
part of the population starts to develop bloat. Some reasons explaining this as a general
tendency are that crossover on average favours smaller than average trees in removal but
not in replacement [40]. Secondly, non-functional parts of a tree can act as a protection
of adverse alterations that crossover can cause [40]. Third, the search space contains more
large trees than small trees [12].
A number of methods exist for mitigating bloat. We describe a few selected methods
here.
One of the simplest ways to combat bloat is to impose a maximum limit on the
depths or number of nodes of the trees in the population and reject oﬀspring created by
evolutionary operators that violate the constraint [40]. This way, the extent to which bloat
can be developed can be limited. A major drawback is, however, that it introduces a hyper-
parameter and choosing a too small limit on the depth or tree size can make it challenging
to properly capture the structure of the data.
Another very prominent approach to limit bloat is by applying parsimony pressure. The
idea is to optimise for a low program size in addition to a high ﬁtness. This can be done by
redeﬁning the ﬁtness function so it incorporates two objectives [40]:
ﬁtnessparsimony = −errortrain −c · size
(2.5)
where errortrain is the training error on the given data set, c ∈R+ is the parsimony coeﬃcient
and size is a model complexity measure such as the number of nodes in the tree. Note that
equation (2.5) is for a problem instance, where the ﬁtness should be maximised, hence
the minus signs in front of the training error and model complexity term, which should
be minimised. Parsimony pressure can also be used explicitly in multi-objective genetic
programming as a separate objective to optimise for in addition to loss.
The hoist mutation operator is also a way to control bloat. By replacing a subtree with
a descendent subtree it leads to smaller trees and will encourage the development of simpler
models [40].
2.2.4
Diversity
Like bloat, diversity is also an important aspect to consider in genetic programming and in
population based evolutionary algorithms in general as insuﬃcient diversity in the population
can lead to premature convergence to a suboptimal solution without suﬃcient exploration
of the search space. Also if the population becomes too genetically similar, many common
evolutionary operators such as subtree crossover continue to produce oﬀspring similar to the
parent making the problem persist [10]. On the other hand it is also important to note that
high diversity is not a guarantee for an ideal search as a high diversity can also mean the
exploration comes at a lack of exploitation of the promising areas of the search space.
Measuring and maintaining the right amount of diversity is still an active research area in
genetic programming and diﬀerent approaches each have their advantages and disadvantages
and work better for some problem domains than others [22, 9, 10]. In the literature we
typically distinguish between structural and behavioural diversity (also known as phenotypic
diversity) [10]. Structural diversity pertains to the diﬀerence in structure of the trees, where
structure can mean aspects such as the size, shape or the functions and terminals used. The
behavioural diversity is the diversity in the behaviour of the programs, motivated by the
fact that two programs that are structurally diﬀerent may still behave similarly and give
the same outputs for the same input [9].
7
==PAGE:16==
2.2
Genetic Programming
2
BACKGROUND

on the runtime of computing them, where N is the number of of individuals in the popula-

metrics from Table 2.1, we will also apply two diversity algorithmic techniques from evolu-
tionary computation literature in the context of the QLattice. We describe these methods
for genetic programming in more detail below below.

Genetic Marker Density
A relatively recent approach by Burks and Punch [10] uses a
structural diversity metric for both measuring and maintaining diversity during search of
a genetic programming algorithm. The method uses the notion of genetic markers which
are deﬁned as the encoding of the structure of a tree at the top levels near the root. The
genetic marker of a tree is computed by a depth-ﬁrst traversal from the root to depth d;
alternatively starting at each of the subtrees at depth d′ and then going down d layers by
a depth-ﬁrst traversal. The string that arises from this traversal by concatenation of the
labels of the nodes in visited order is the genetic marker of the given tree for depth d. Figure
2.5 shows an example. The density of a genetic marker is then the proportion of trees with
that genetic marker in the population computed using the formula:
ρm = ∥Pm∥
|P| , Pm = {∀p ∈P | M(p) = m}
(2.6)
Finally the level of diversity for a population of trees is given by the maximum density of
the genetic markers of the population.
Based on the genetic markers, the method for maintaining diversity keeps a Pareto
front of solutions that is updated every generation as follows: In each generation a new

Figure 2.5: Example of computing the genetic marker of four genetic programming trees. Left: The genetic
markers for tree 1, 2 and 3 are computed for depth d = 1 starting at the root level. Tree 1 and 2 have the
same genetic marker even though they diﬀer in the right most leaf node. Right: The genetic marker of tree
4 for depth d = 1 starting at level 1 in the tree. Because the root has two subtrees the genetic marker of
tree 4 becomes the concatenation of the genetic markers of the two subtrees. Example from [10].
population of individuals is generated through the evolutionary operators as normal and
the genetic marker for each individual is computed. Together with the previous population
this gives 2N individuals in total.
Pareto tournament selection is used repeatedly until
only N individuals remain, which then form the new population of the generation. Pareto
tournament selection is a multi-objective version of standard tournament selection, where
the result of the tournament consists of the individuals which are not Pareto dominated by
any other individual in the tournament. An individual A is said to dominate individual B
if and only if A is at least equal to B in all objectives and A is strictly better than B in
at least one objective [10]. The two objectives here are the ﬁtness and the genetic marker
density of the population.
By taking the density into account during search the algorithm encourages that diﬀerent
structures near the root of the tree are maintained, which the authors argue will preempt
premature convergence of the search. The algorithm does not incur asymptotic overhead as
it requires linear time in the size of a tree to compute its genetic marker, which has to be
done only once for each individual in the population.
Novelty Search:
Novelty search is an algorithmic technique developed by Lehman and
Stanley [30] that takes the promotion of diversity to the extreme by completely disregarding
the ﬁtness of solutions during search and only use diversity as objective. The technique was
ﬁrst used with the NEAT algorithm [49] from the ﬁeld of Neuroevolution, but the authors
have used it with success for genetic programming as well [29]. The main motivation behind
the technique is that it mitigates deception in the search space (i.e. local suboptimal solutions
that can be diﬃcult too break out of). Apart from being a very interesting idea in its own
right, multiple studies in Neuroevolution [33, 51, 18, 48] have since built upon the idea
and further showed how eﬀective incorporating novelty during search can be. Recently a
paper using MAP-Elites (which uses novelty search) and the state-of-the-art evolutionary
algorithm CMA-ES for continuous optimisation also apply it in the context of symbolic
regression [8].
Novelty search for genetic programming works by running a genetic programming algo-
rithm as normal with the following two modiﬁcations. First, the ﬁtness of each individual
is substituted for a novelty score. The novelty of an individual x can be computed by the
sparseness metric ρ:
ρ(x) = 1
k
k
X
i=0
dist(x, µi)
(2.7)
where µi is the ith-nearest neighbour of x with respect to the distance metric dist, which
is a domain-dependent measure of the behavioural diversity between two individuals in the
9
==PAGE:18==
2.3
The QLattice
2
BACKGROUND
search space. Secondly, in addition to the individuals of the current population, the set of
neighbours to compare with include past individuals maintained during search stored in a
so-called archive (implemented by e.g. a set type data structure). The archive is initially
empty and individuals are then added probabilistically during each generation if not already
present. The authors emphasise that the novelty metric should characterise how far away
an individual is from the rest of the population in terms of its behaviour. For some domains
such as robot walk and maze ﬁnding, measuring diﬀerence in behaviour is more obvious than
for others types of domains. For symbolic regression one could use one of the behaviour type
diversity metrics from Table 2.1 [29].
2.3
The QLattice
The QLattice (short for Quantum Lattice) is a supervised machine learning algorithm for
symbolic regression developed by the AI company Abzu [2]. The idea behind the QLattice
is inspired by the path integral formulation of Richard Feynman [3, 41] by sampling the
theoretically inﬁnite list of possible mathematical expressions that link a set of features X
to a response y and interpret the expressions as spatial paths between X and y [56]. Inter-
actions among spatial paths then give rise to the operations that make up the mathematical
expressions that model the data.
QLattice
feyn
data
generated graphs
[g1, g2, g3, ...]
Client side
Server side
n best graphs
[g'1, g'2, ..., g'n]
Figure 2.6: Depiction of the communication between
the client and server when running the QLattice al-
gorithm.
During training the best graphs from the
QGraph head are sent to the server and new graphs are
generated and received from the server [41].
The QLattice algorithm consists of two
main components:
A QLattice and a
QGraph. 1 The QLattice is a data structure
mimicking a quantum lattice from quantum
ﬁeld theory. It is 50 by 8 by 8 in dimen-
sions in the x, y and z directions, respec-
tively, but wraps around like a torus along
the x axis [3]. The QGraph is a conceptu-
ally inﬁnite list of graphs sorted by perfor-
mance evaluated on the training data, but
in practice the user only operates with a ﬁ-
nite list of graphs corresponding to the head
of the QGraph. Each graph in the QGraph is
a model of the data and represents a math-
ematical formula.
Each graph also corresponds to a path in the QLattice as each node
has a location within the QLattice, which reﬂects its place in the search space. As a node
represents an interaction of particles along a path, the nodes in the context of the QLattice
algorithm are called interactions. The locations of the nodes in the best graphs found during
training also aﬀect the probability distribution in the QLattice for generating new graphs
such that better graphs become more likely. The nodes in each graph are either input nodes,
internal nodes or the output node of the graph. The input nodes contain features from the
training data, the internal nodes contain one of the mathematical functions listed in Table
?? in the appendix and the output node gives the target. The input nodes and output node
each have a weight and bias term, which are changed during training using stochastic gradi-
ent descent. The input nodes also have a scale to accommodate diﬀerent scales of features
in the data [1, 41].
As of writing the QLattice component is running on a high performance computing
cluster in Munich in Germany and the user of the algorithm interacts with it through the
interface provided in the feyn Python module [3, 41], which handles the communication with
the server using the HTTPS protocol (see Figure 2.6). The only data being sent from client
to server are the n best performing graphs extracted from the head of the QGraph in each
1Note that the term QLattice can refer to both the algorithm and the QLattice component of the
algorithm. When referring to the QLattice component we will strive for marking with verbatim unless in a
ﬁgure or if its clear from the context.
10

Machine Learning Essentials

iteration, which the QLattice uses for updating the probability distribution for generating
new graphs. In the physics analogy this corresponds to increasing the probability for the
corresponding particle trajectories.
The training data therefore only resides client side,
which ensures conﬁdentiality and user control of the data [1, 41]. More details are given in
Section 4.1.
2.4
Machine Learning Essentials
The main type of machine learning task in this project is supervised learning, in which the
goal is to learn a function (model) that maps a set of input features X (predictors) to an
output y (response) [7, 19]. The primary goal is generally to ﬁnd a model that generalises
well to new unseen data based on the training data available. At the same time it is also
desirable to ﬁnd simple models, which are easy to interpret and use for understanding the
relationship between the features and the output .
2.4.1
Performance Metrics
Beginning with regression, the standard way to evaluate the predictive performance of a
model is to compute the loss L(t, ˆf(x)) incurred by comparing the model prediction ˆf(x)
with the target value t for a number of observations x of a data set D. Using the deﬁnition of
expectation, the expected loss is given by summing over the loss L(t, ˆf(x)) times probability
p(x, t) of observing the value pair x and t for all possible values [7]:
E[L] =
Z Z
L(t, ˆf(x))p(x, t) dx dt
(2.8)
Squared Error Loss:
There are many choices to the loss function L to use. For many
machine learning models we use squared error loss for regression. This is also known as
residual sum of squares (RSS) and is given by [7]:
RSS =
N
X
n=1
(yn −ˆyn)2
(2.9)
where N is the number of observations in the data set evaluated on, yn is the target value
of observation n and ˆyn is the prediction of observation n. The reasoning behind squaring is
to penalise large diﬀerences more heavily than smaller diﬀerences. If we drop the exponent
and sum the absolute diﬀerences the loss would be absolute error loss instead [7]:
lossabsolute error =
N
X
n=1
|yn −ˆyn|
(2.10)
Under squared error loss one can prove that the optimal value for ˆf(x) is E[t|x] [7] i.e. the
target value we expect for a given observation, which ﬁts well with our intuition. Substituting
ˆf(x) = E[t|x] into (2.8) the expected loss can be decomposed into two terms:
E[L] =
Z
( ˆf(x) −E[t|x])2p(x)dx +
Z
(E[t|x] −t)2p(x)dx
(2.11)
Observe now that the function ˆf(x), which we wish to determine, is only present in the ﬁrst
term and was minimised by ˆf(x) = E[t|x], making the term vanish. Thus, the expected loss
for the best possible model is given by the degree to which the expected value of the target
diﬀers by the target value in the data evaluated on. In practice there is always going to
be some intrinsic noise in the data causing this diﬀerence and highlights the important fact
that we should not expect to be able to achieve a loss of 0 when the model is evaluated on
unseen data [7].

Coeﬃcient of Determination:
Another performance metric common for regression re-
lated to the residual sum of squares is the coeﬃcient of determination, R2, which can be
practical when interpreting the loss of models. This is given by [7]:
R2 = 1 −RSS
TSS
(2.12)
where TSS = P
i(yi −¯a)2 (total sum of squares) and RSS is as above. The R2 can be
interpreted as the proportion of the variance in the target value t that is predicted based
on x. R2 lies in a range ] −∞, 1] with 1 being a perfect ﬁt and 0 being as good as always
outputting the average of the target values of the data, which can serve as a baseline.
Squared error loss and the R2 score are applicable for classiﬁcation, but are less well
suited than for regression since the targets are numeric representations of classes, where
the order is not necessarily meaningful. Other performance metrics are therefore generally
preferred for classiﬁcation problems [7]. Below we list a number of these that we will be
using in Section 7 when evaluating the algorithms on classiﬁcation test cases.
Binary Cross-Entropy
The term cross-entropy comes from information theory and can
be used to measure similarity between two probability distributions. For classiﬁcation we
compare the estimated probabilities with the empirically observed in the training data and
can thereby compute the likelihood of the data coming from the estimated probability dis-
tributions. The larger the likelihood the better. Similarly, the cross-entropy loss is then
deﬁned as the negative log likelihood where the minus sign makes it into a minimisation
problem and the logarithm simpliﬁes computations without aﬀecting the monotonicity of
the loss function. In the binary case the cross-entropy is therefore given by [7]:
lossbinary-cross-entropy = −1
N
N
X


tn log ˆf(xn) + (1 −tn) log(1 −ˆf(xn))

(2.13)
Precision and Recall
Precision and recall are deﬁned as:
Precision =
TP
TP + FP
(2.14)
Recall =
TP
TP + FN
(2.15)
where TP, FP, FN are the number of true positives, false positives and false negatives,
respectively. The precision tells us the proportion of positive identiﬁcations that are correct.
Recall is the proportion of actual positives that identiﬁed correctly as positives. The interval
of possible values for these metrics is [0, 1] where 1 is the best [13].
F1 Score
The F1 score combines precision and recall in one metric and is given by the
harmonic mean of the two:
F1 = 2 · Precision · Recall
Precision + Recall
(2.16)
The fact that the F1 score gives equal importance to precision and recall has drawbacks,
however: In practice, diﬀerent types of mis-classiﬁcations will incur diﬀerent cost.
The
canonical example is detection of fraud or medical illness where there is a diﬀerence in
importance of erring with false negatives and false positives [20].
Matthew’s Correlation Coeﬃcient
Matthew’s Correlation Coeﬃcient (MCC) is de-
ﬁned as:
MCC =
TP × TN −FP × FN
p
(TP + FP)(TP + FN)(TN + FP)(TN + FN)
(2.17)
and does not suﬀer from the drawback of the F1 score to the same extent while still capturing
more than precision and recall do each one their own [7].

==PAGE:21==
2.4
Machine Learning Essentials
2
BACKGROUND
AUC
The receiver operating characteristic (ROC) curve is given by the true positive rate
(TPR) along the 2nd axis against the false positive rate (FPR) along the 1st axis. The
area under the ROC curve abbreviated AUC is then the performance measure. The area
will be a value between 0 and 1 with 1 being perfect classiﬁcation of all observations and
an area of 1
2 corresponding to random guessing on average. The AUC has the advantage
of being classiﬁcation-threshold-invariant meaning the performance of the model can be
measured irrespective of what the threshold of the output probability (assuming a binary
classiﬁer) yielding a positive class is set to. Often the threshold is set to θ = 0.5 but it can
be advantageous to set it to some diﬀerent value θ ∈[0, 1] sometimes. On the other hand,
if one type of classiﬁcation error is considerably worse than the opposite then the AUC is
less useful because of said threshold-invariance. For instance failing to detect a disease for
a patient is much worse than mistakenly thinking a healthy patient is ill [7].
2.4.2
Model Selection
Model selection is the task of selecting the best performing model among a set of candidate
models with respect to a performance metric evaluated on a given data set
[19, 7]. The
training error is rarely used for choosing the best model since models that are very ﬂexible
can easily perform well on the training data but not generalise well to unseen data. Instead
other model selection schemes are preferred. We brieﬂy list some of the ones we are going
to use in this project below.
Note that we only consider model selection here and not
the computation of an unbiased estimate of the generalisation error after having selected a
model.
Train-Test Split:
A simple way to do model selection is to randomly split the data avail-
able into a train and test set for ﬁtting the models and choosing the best model, respectively.
To avoid the split having too much inﬂuence, often multiple random splits are made and
averaged over, which is sometimes called Monte Carlo cross-validation [7].
Cross-Validation:
A common alternative is K-fold cross-validation. The data is split into
K folds and each fold is then used as the test data while the rest is used for training the
models. The test error is computed for each model for each fold and the average test error
across the folds is then used for selecting the best model [19, 7].
Information Criteria:
Lastly we mention here a diﬀerent kind of approach, which es-
timates the generalisation error of a model based on the training error errortrain and the
so-called optimism op. The in-sample error can then be computed as Errin = errortrain +op,
which gives a good estimate of the extra-sample error and thereby the generalisation er-
ror. We omit the theoretical details here, but present below the formula for the two most
common information criteria which rely on this idea [19].
The Akaike information criterion (AIC) can be formulated as: [19]
AIC = 2k −2 ln(bL)
(2.18)
When training a model based using least squares ﬁtting, AIC becomes:
AIC = 2k + n ln(RSS)
(2.19)
where an extra term n ln n + 2C, C ∈R has been omitted since we are only interested in
relative diﬀerences in AIC between models and this term does not change between models
for the same data.
Similarly we can formulate the Bayesian information criterion (BIC): [19]
BIC = k ln(n) −2 ln(bL)
(2.20)
13
==PAGE:22==
2.4
Machine Learning Essentials
2
BACKGROUND
It is very common to assume that the residuals are independent and identically Gaussian
distributed with mean 0. Under this assumption and when the model is trained based on
squared error loss then we can calculate BIC as:
BIC = k ln(n) + n ln(RSS/n)
(2.21)
Information Criteria for the QLattice
AIC and BIC can be used with the QLattice
when determining which graphs in the head of the QGraph are the best based on the training
error and model complexity. This is achieved by computing the information criterion value
based on either formula (2.19) or (2.21) with the parameter count k being the sum of
parameter counts for the function operators in the graph. Table 2.7 shows the parameter
count for each function in the set of functions used in the QLattice algorithm. Figure 2.8
shows an example of computing the parameter count for a graph. The parameter counts
have been chosen to reﬂect the ﬂexibility an operator gives a model and to work well in
practice with the information criteria. For example, the gaussian function with one input
x can be broken down into three sequential sub-operations x′ = x2, x′′ = −x′ and exp(x′′) so
the parameter count has therefore been set to 3. Note that the mean and standard deviation
of the gaussian function are static here with values 0 and 1, respectively, and therefore do
not contribute to the parameter count [56].

2.4.3
Bias-Variance Decomposition
So far we have considered how to use a performance metric to determine how good a model is
and how to choose the best model among a set of candidates. In order to better analyse and
understand what makes a model good at making predictions, however, the theory behind
the bias-variance trade-oﬀis also important to mention. Later in Section 7.2.3 we will also
use this for the QLattice for a synthetic data set.
Standard Formulation
Recall that under squared error loss the expected loss E[L] was
given in equation (2.11) and we can only do our best to minimise the ﬁrst term (the reducible
loss). In equation (2.11) we integrated over the entire solution space in the ﬁrst term. Let
us now instead base our expectation of the reducible loss on a data set D with a limited
number of observations. Let y(x; D) denote the prediction of a model trained on data set D
for input x and let h(x) in the following be the output of the true function underlying the
data.
2 The expected value of the integrand now becomes: ED[{y(x; D) −h(x)}2]. Using
2We use a slightly diﬀerent notation in this subsection compared to Section 2.4.1 for ease of reference to
[39] of the extended bias-variance decomposition that we will use.

the rule E[(a −b)2] = (E[a] −b)2 + E[(a −E[b]])2] for expectation, where a and b are random
variables, this can be written as two terms [39]:
ED[{y(x; D) −h(x)}2]
= {ED[y(x; D)] −h(x)}2

{z

(bias)
2
+ ED[{y(x; D) −ED[h(x)]}2]
|
{z
}
variance
(2.22)
which is the squared bias and the variance of the model. The bias is the systematic error the
model makes and an unbiased model would be a model that is right on average. We can see
that it represents the extent to which the average prediction over all data sets diﬀers from
the true underlying function. The variance is the ﬂexibility of the model and the degree to
which the values for individual data sets predicted by a model vary around their average. A
low variance model would often give the same value and how close that value is depends on
the bias of the model [7, 19].
The important point here is that to achieve a low generalisation error, a model has to
have a good trade-oﬀbetween the bias and variance as decreasing the bias may incur an
increase in variance and vice versa.
In the context of genetic programming one way to
decrease the variance of the model is to use a small function set [39]. Increasing the higher
parsimony pressure or using a lower population size may also help, but comes at the cost
increasing the bias.
Extended Bias-Variance Decomposition
In a recent paper Owen et al. [39] extend
the standard bias-variance decomposition from above by splitting the variance term into
two terms: The internal variance and the external variance. The internal variance is the
error associated with random actions in the algorithm itself and the external variance stems
from limited sampling of the problem i.e. error from only having a data set with a limited
number of observations. Splitting the variance into these two terms makes the bias-variance
decomposition more detailed when dealing with stochastic algorithms such as genetic pro-
gramming. Let S be the stochastic eﬀect of the model, so the choice of model y(x; D, S) is
now dependent of both D and S. S could for instance represent the seed for the random
number generator. We then consider the expectation of over D and S. The extended form
of the reducible loss under squared error loss can be shown to then be:
ED,S

==PAGE:25==
3
RELATED WORK
3
Related Work
In this section we present four machine learning algorithms operating in the symbolic regres-
sion space, two of which we are going to compare the QLattice with in this project (Section
3.1). We also describe recent related work on benchmarking symbolic regression methods
(Section 3.2).
3.1
Evolutionary Algorithms for Symbolic Regression
GPLearn:
GPLearn [50] is an open source genetic programming algorithm for symbolic
regression written in Python with a relatively high number of stars and forks on GitHub3
(940 stars and 160 forks as of May 2021). GPLearn includes most of common evolutionary
operators mentioned in Section 2.2 and has a simple interface for training a model on both
regression and binary classiﬁcation problems. GPLearn is open source, which makes it easy
to run locally and integrate with other tests and will be used as the main genetic program-
ming algorithm to compare the QLattice with when studying similarities and diﬀerences to
genetic programming.
Eureqa:
Eureqa [46] is a proprietary symbolic regression framework using genetic pro-
gramming originally developed by Michael Schmidt and Hod Lipson, which has later been
acquired by Datarobot [11, 24]. Eureqa is considered a state-of-the-art symbolic regression
algorithm and has been used in multiple scientiﬁc studies and Fortune 500 companies in
the industry [5, 57, 54]. This makes Eureqa very interesting to compare the performance of
QLattice with.
An interesting aspect of Eureqa is that it has a very large function set compared to
the QLattice and GPLearn and the function set also includes logical boolean functions and
functions like max, modulo, absolute value and rounding (see Table A.1).
AI-Feynman:
Like the QLattice, AI Feynman [54] is an algorithm for symbolic regression
that draws inspiration from physics.
The algorithm was ﬁrst designed by Silviu-Marian
Udrescu and Max Tegmark at MIT in 2020 and since then improved in version 2.0 [53]. The
primary purpose has been to eﬃciently uncover formulas from physics based on synthetic
data samples generated using the underlying formula mimicking measurements from an
experiment. For this reason the algorithm is heavily geared towards being able to recognise
the types of structures often seen in formulas from physics and in [54] the authors only
benchmark it on data generated from physical formulas. The scope and aim of the algorithm
can therefore be said to be somewhat less general than the QLattice. The algorithm works
by performing a number of steps, each of which is aimed at uncovering a certain kind of
structure in the data. This includes dimensional analysis, ﬁtting a low degree polynomial,
brute-force search, training a neural network and retrying the entire process multiple times
with a transformed data sets e.g. by including fewer variables from the data [54].
Although there is a Python API for the algorithm4, the interface is limited, which makes
it challenging to integrate into a combined test with the QLattice, GPLearn and Eureqa.
Furthermore, because AI Feynman performs multiple resource intensive search steps such as
training a relatively large neural network it is also very time consuming to run without high-
end hardware. After some preliminary testing with the algorithm, the choice was therefore
made to not include it as a part of this project and instead put focus elsewhere.
TuringBot:
TuringBot [47] is like Eureqa a proprietary evolutionary symbolic regression
algorithm. The TuringBot algorithm uses simulated annealing instead of genetic program-
3https://github.com/trevorstephens/gplearn (accessed January 2021)
4https://pypi.org/project/aifeynman (accessed February 2021)
17
==PAGE:26==
3.2
Benchmarking Symbolic Regression Algorithms
3
RELATED WORK
ming for driving the search, however. The system can be download in a limited free version
or a paid version which has more functionalities. It has a graphical user interface and a
basic Python API. Since the system is proprietary and not based on genetic programming,
the choice was made to not compare the QLattice with this algorithm in this project either.
3.2
Benchmarking Symbolic Regression Algorithms
A common challenge in the selection, development and comparison of machine learning
algorithms is gathering and preprocessing the data to use for training and evaluation. Many
data sets exist publicly available on the web, but often do not follow the same standard.
Motivated by this Olson et al. [37] have created a comprehensive data set suite called the
Penn Machine Learning Benchmark (abbreviated PMLB) consisting of 136 regression and
162 classiﬁcation data sets. The data sets come from common sources including the UCL
ML repository5 and Kaggle6 and have been preprocessed so they follow the same streamlined
format making it easy to work with them. The authors have also documented that there is
diversity in the structure of the data sets, so evaluating models and comparing performance
should give useful and representative results. We will use the PMLB data set suite in this
project for evaluating the QLattice and comparing with genetic programming in Section
7.3 and 7.4. In Section 5.2.1 we will also consider the PMLB data sets in more detail and
select a subset to work with. Another interesting aspect of the work of Olson et al. is the
website associated with the study [42], which allows the user to interactively browse the
data sets and see more details about them such as summary statistics, feature correlation
and distributions. The website is made using Dash and has provided some inspiration for
part of the work with making the visualisation tool of this project (refer to Section 5.1 and
6).
Similar to some of the goals of this project, Orzechowski et al. [38] have carried out
a study where they benchmark four genetic programming symbolic regression algorithms
on the PMLB data set suite. The algorithms are Multiple regression genetic programming
(MRGP) [4], ε-Lexicase selection (EPLEX) [27], Age-ﬁtness Pareto Optimization (AFP) [45]
(developed by the same authors Schmidt and Lipson, who started Eureqa) and Geometric
Semantic Genetic Programming (GSGP) [32]. In addition to this they compare these with
nine traditional machine learning approaches from scikit-learn7 including gradient boosting
methods like xgboost and gradient boost. Their results show that the genetic programming
algorithms perform strongly compared to the best traditional machine learning approaches.
In particular EPLEX and MRGP do very well in terms of predictive performance on the test
sets. The results also show a drawback of the genetic programming algorithms, however,
which is that that they are considerably slower in terms of CPU time than for instance xgboost
and gradient boost which also do well performance wise on the test set. The approach used
for evaluating the algorithms is to average test errors over all the data sets for each algorithm
and compare the ranked order among the algorithms with respect to the test error. The test
error is computed using a two-layer evaluation scheme with Monte-Carlo train-test splitting
for the outer layer and 5-fold cross-validation for the inner layer. This allows for both tuning
hyper-parameters for each model and for making an unbiased comparison of performance
between the algorithms. We will use a similar approach in this project in 7.4 and describe
the setup in more detail in Section 5.2.
Orzechowski et al.
[38] did not include Eureqa in their benchmark and neither did
another recent study by Jan ˇZegklitz and Petr Poˇs´ık [57] on benchmarking state-of-the-art
symbolic regression algorithms with the reason that the software is proprietary. Udrescu
et al. [54] compare the performance of AI Feynman with Eureqa, where they come to the
conclusion that Eureqa is able to solve 71 % of their test cases while AI-Feynman solves
5https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/index.php (accessed March 2021)
6https://www.kaggle.com (accessed March 2021)
7https://scikit-learn.org (accessed March 2021)
18
==PAGE:27==
3.2
Benchmarking Symbolic Regression Algorithms
3
RELATED WORK
100 %. They only run the algorithms on synthetic test cases generated by formulas from
physics, however. Ashok et al. [5] who have developed the Auxiliary Truth enhanced Genetic
Algorithm compare their algorithm with Eureqa and with TuringBot and show that their
algorithm when utilising knowledge of the structure of data is able to outperform both of
the two algorithms in some cases. Finally, we mention here the recent work of Wilstrup
and Kasak [56], who benchmark the QLattice on PMLB data sets like in this project. They
compare the QLattice with popular traditional machine learning frameworks such as linear
regression, random forest and gradient boosting and their study shows that for small data
sets, the QLattice as a symbolic regression algorithm, generalises the best to out-of-sample
data among the methods tested.

==PAGE:28==
4
ANALYSIS
4
Analysis
We now make an initial analysis of some of the aspects of the project. First we analyse the
QLattice algorithm in evolutionary terms and how it compares with genetic programming.
Most of the information is gathered from reading the documentation [1] and inspection of the
feyn source code [3]; some details are also from discussions with Abzu. Next we present three
problems from the genetic programming literature, which are useful in analysing the runtime
of genetic programming algorithms and understanding their working principles. Some known
asymptotic bounds on the runtime for three simple genetic programming algorithms are also
listed. In order to gain more insight into how quickly and to what extent we can make the
QLattice develop a speciﬁc kind of structure we will later encode the problems for the
QLattice and compare with three simple genetic programming algorithms (see Section 7.1)
for which known runtime bounds have been derived, which we also brieﬂy list in that section.
4.1
The QLattice as an Evolutionary Algorithm
Fitting Loop
Despite the idea for the QLattice algorithm described in Section 2.3 draw-
ing inspiration from quantum mechanics, the way the algorithm works has many things
in common with a population based evolutionary algorithm and genetic programming in
particular. A diagram showing the main steps of the training process is seen in Figure 4.1.
• Initialisation: The QLattice is initialised and the QGraph will consist of random graphs
in the search space for the training data corresponding to an initial population of ran-
dom trees in genetic programming.
• Step 1: Next, ﬁtting the QGraph head on the training data corresponds to evaluating
the individuals in the population of a genetic programming algorithm by computing
their ﬁtness. For the QLattice algorithm this is done by ﬁrst using stochastic gradient
descent on each graph in the QGraph to tune the weights and bias terms. Stochastic
gradient descent, in short, works by computing the gradient of the weights with re-
spect to the loss function (which for the QLattice is squared error loss for regression
and binary cross-entropy for classiﬁcation) evaluated on a mini-batch of the training
observations and update the weights by a coeﬃcient η times the negative gradient [36].
By default the performance metric is squared error loss and binary cross-entropy for
regression and classiﬁcation, respectively. Afterwards the QGraph head is sorted based
on either training loss, AIC or BIC.
• Step 2: The second step of the ﬁtting loop where the best graphs are extracted can
be seen as the selection step in genetic programming and the chosen individuals will
then aﬀect the next generation. For the QLattice algorithm this happens by updating
the probability distribution in the QLattice, whereas for genetic programming this is
done by means of the crossover and mutation evolutionary operators.
• Step 3: The selection scheme from genetic programming most closely mimicking the
QLattice is arguably truncation selection, where the top most ﬁt individuals are chosen
for deﬁning the next population with the slight diﬀerence that the QLattice imposes
a restriction on how close the top graphs can be to each other in the QLattice (more
on this below) when extracting the best graphs.
• Step 4: Before repeating the ﬁtting loop in the next iteration, new graphs are gener-
ated in the QLattice and appended to the head of the QGraph (step four of the ﬁtting
loop). The head of the QGraph at this point then corresponds to the new population
20
==PAGE:29==
4.1
The QLattice as an Evolutionary Algorithm
4
ANALYSIS
for the next generation in genetic programming. Note that the QGraph head does not
only contain new graphs, but also graphs from the previous iteration. This can also
be the case for genetic programming if individuals are replicated to the new population.
• Termination: Finally after training is ﬁnished, the ﬁrst graph in the head of the sorted
QGraph head is the ﬁnal model and used for making predictions.
Step 1: Fit QGraph
head on training data
Initialise QLattice and
extract head of QGraph
Step 3: Update
QLattice with best
graphs from QGraph
Step 2: Extract best
graphs from QGraph
head
Make predictions based
on best graph
Step 4: Extract new
graphs from the QLattice
and add to QGraph head
Figure 4.1: Flowchart showing the main steps of the QLattice algorithm. The four steps in the middle make
up the ﬁtting loop of the algorithm, which is repeated a number of iterations [3].
Closure and Suﬃciency
Like genetic programming, the QLattice also uses protected
versions of some of the functions in the function set to avoid numerical instability when
evaluating the graphs. The two protected functions are seen below:
log(x) :=
(
log(10−5)
if x < 10−5
log x
otherwise
(4.1)
1
x :=
(
1
0.05·sign(x)
if x < |0.05|
1
x
otherwise
(4.2)
Note that this diﬀers slightly from what Koza and GPLearn use (see Section 2.2). Also,
a function like the square root is not protected by taking the absolute value of the input.
Because the QLattice operates with a theoretically inﬁnite list of graphs, it is not problematic
if some graphs give NaN loss values (invalid numerical values) as they will be discarded and
other graphs will rise to the head of the QGraph in the next iteration.
Concerning suﬃciency, the function set of the QLattice includes the most common arith-
metic functions, which can model many kind of structures (refer to appendix A). Unlike
genetic programming, subtraction is not directly included as a function in the function set,
but can still be achieved using e.g. a linear interaction with a negative weight and then
an add function.
Diversity Through Graph Locations
The QLattice uses a number of methods for
maintaining diversity during training. One of these techniques is to take the output location
of the graphs into account when ﬁtting and extracting the best graphs from the QGraph
head in ﬁtting loop step 1 and step 2, respectively. In step 1 after the QGraph head has been
sorted, some graphs are discarded. Identical graphs with the same hash code are discarded
as they are redundant and graphs whose output interaction x-location is the same as density
number of other graphs with lower loss are also discarded. The default value for the density
is 50. The QLattice also applies an extinction event to all the graphs at a certain x-location,
which starts at 0 at the start of the algorithm and is increased by 1 every second iteration
(marked by the barren attribute in the feyn. qgraph class). When it exceeds the width of
50 of the QLattice it wraps around and starts at 0 again. All graphs with x-location equal to
the x-value currently targeted by the extinction event are discarded, which should help avoid
21
==PAGE:30==
4.1
The QLattice as an Evolutionary Algorithm
4
ANALYSIS
local minima. One exception to this is that if the x-location is updated to have x-location
equal to one of the top 3 best graphs in the QGraph head the x-location is re-updated by
incrementing by 1 such that it skips the x-location of the top 3 best graphs. This way the
best graphs are protected against the extinction event. Finally graph decay described in a
following paragraph will also discard a number of graphs in this ﬁtting loop step.
In step 2 of the ﬁtting loop when the n best graphs are extracted from the QGraph head,
the graphs are chosen in sorted order subject to the constraint that the x-location of the
output interaction for a graph is at least dist max away from the x-location of a better graph.
The default value for dist max is 3. Note that the distance metric computes the distance
modulo the width of the QLattice such that it wraps around. This will make the n best
graphs extracted have x-location at a more diverse set of lattice locations in the QLattice,
which will make for a more diverse set of graphs to update the QLattice graph probability
distribution with. The idea behind this technique is reminiscent of island modelling for
evolutionary algorithms (as described in for instance [52]), where separate populations are
evolved during training to promote diversity among other things.
It is also reminiscent
of operating with species of individuals in a population as used in for instance the NEAT
algorithm [49]. The dist max hyper-parameter does not have a name in the source code,
so we make the choice of referring to this parameter as dist max or the island maximum
distance in this project.
Graph Decay
In addition to discarding graphs at the same location, the QLattice al-
gorithm also uses graph decay to discard graphs in step 1 of the ﬁtting loop. Initially the
age of each graph in the QGraph head is set to zero and the age is increased by one every
iteration. After sorting the QGraph head, graphs whose age exceed a certain age threshold
are discarded. The threshold depends on the number (count) of graphs, cnt, with output
interaction at a certain location in the QLattice and is computed based on the following
half-life formula:
A0 · e−
cnt
density·n·τ + 1
(4.3)
where n, τ, A0, density ∈R are hyper-parameters with default values: A0 = 200, n = 20, τ =
0.693, density = 50. Figure 4.2 gives some intuition into how the graph decay is aﬀected by
the hyper-parameters. Lowering A0 lowers the starting point. Lowering n, τ or the density
lowers the decay rate so the cut oﬀpoint for the graph age will decrease with cnt more
slowly. Graph decay will help prevent premature convergence by allowing newer graphs to
take the place of older graphs. This parameter is also similar to the notion of stagnation in
diversity maintaining evolutionary algorithms.

Mutation Strength
In evolutionary algorithms for continuous optimisation, it is common
to have a mutation strength parameter, which determines the degree to which changes to the
weights of the problem is made. The mutation strength can either be static or dynamically
changed as part of the run of the algorithm [16]. The QLattice has a similar feature as each
interaction of a graph has an associated strength value that inﬂuences the impact that that
interaction has on updating the probability distribution of the QLattice if the graph is sent
to the QLattice in step 3 of the ﬁtting loop. By default the mutation strength is 1, but can
either be manually set to something else or set to the signal of each interaction w.r.t. the
Pearson correlation computed based on some data supplied.
Diﬀerences from Genetic Programming
The aspects described above show that the
QLattice employs many of the same ideas as in genetic programming, but also that the
QLattice achieves the same goals slightly diﬀerently.
Another diﬀerence is the graph models themselves. In genetic programming the graphs
are trees, but the graphs generated by the QLattice need not be, since the output from
one node can be the input for two or more nodes like for instance seen in Figure 4.3. One
advantage of allowing this is that it makes it possible to reuse structures in the graph and
make it smaller and easier to interpret.
The QLattice and genetic programming also diﬀer in how scalars can become part of the
models. While this happens through the application of the evolutionary operators in genetic
programming by choosing random ephemeral scalars or scalars from a set of predeﬁned
values, the QLattice instead tunes a weight, bias and scale term associated with with every
input, output and linear interaction.
Finally, a noteworthy diﬀerence is that the population size in genetic programming is
often of ﬁxed size. For the QLattice the number of graphs in the QGraph head changes
slightly between iterations depending on how many graphs are discarded at step 1 of the
ﬁtting loop.
4.2
Theoretical Problems from GP Literature
In runtime analysis, which we allude to here, we are interested in analysing theoretically and
empirically the computational complexity of an evolutionary algorithm and derive upper and
lower bounds on the runtime when it is run on certain types of problems. Such analyses can
help characterise the eﬃciency of an algorithm and increase the understanding of its working
principles [6]. We will here consider three problems from genetic programming literature:
The Order problem, the Majority problem and the MAX problem. For these problems
there exist a number of proved runtime bounds for simple genetic programming algorithms
[31]. We present some of these results here and later we will implement and evaluate the
23
==PAGE:32==
4.2
Theoretical Problems from GP Literature
4
ANALYSIS
runtime empirically (refer to Section 7.1) and also make an attempt to run the QLattice on
the problems in a similar manner.
The three simple genetic algorithms we will use as a reference point for the QLattice
are the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP and SMO-GP. The (1+1) GP is shown in algorithm 4.1 for
a maximisation problem, where f denotes the ﬁtness function. The HVL-Prime mutation
operator it uses is shown in 4.3. RLS-GP is as a variant of (1+1) GP where k is ﬁxed with
value 1. Further, (1+1) GP* and RLS-GP* denote the strictly elitist variants of the (1+1)
GP and RLS-GP , where the oﬀspring X′ is only accepted to replace the current solution
X if the ﬁtness is strictly better than that of X. The SMO-GP (algorithm 4.2) is a simple
multi-objective genetic programming algorithm that maintains a Pareto front P of solutions.
We are going to use two objectives: The ﬁtness and model size, which should be maximised
and minimised, respectively. In every iteration a new oﬀspring Y is created. If no current
solution in P Pareto dominates Y then it is added to the frontier and all solutions in the
frontier which Y weakly dominates are removed. The deﬁnition of the Pareto dominance
operator is given in deﬁnition 1.
Deﬁnition 1. Let X, Y be solutions, F the ﬁtness function and C the model complexity
[34].
1. Y weakly dominates X (denoted by Y ⪰X) iﬀF(Y ) ≥F(X) ∧C(Y ) ≤C(X)
2. Y dominates X (denoted by Y ≻X) iﬀ(Y ⪰X) ∧(F(Y ) > F(X) ∨C(Y ) < C(X))
3. X and Y are incomparable if neither X ⪰Y nor Y ⪰X
Algorithm 4.1 The (1+1) GP [31]

2: for t ←1, 2, . . . do

Algorithm 4.2 The SMO-GP [31]
1: Initialise a tree X
2: Set P := {X}
3: for t ←1, 2, . . . do
4:
Choose X ∈P uniformly at random
5:

if {Z ∈P|Z ≻Y } = ∅then
7:

8:
end if
9: end for
4.2.1
Order and Majority
The Order and Majority problems have been designed to give insight into the evolution
of tree structure with speciﬁc properties. The ﬁtness is given by the structure of the tree
rather than the exact formula relating the inputs and the outputs. The deﬁnitions of Order
and Majority are seen below:
24
==PAGE:33==
4.2
Theoretical Problems from GP Literature
4
ANALYSIS
Algorithm 4.3 HVL-Prime Mutation Operator [31]
Data: A binary syntax tree X.
Choose op ∈{INS, DEL, SUB} uniformly at random;
if if X is an empty tree then
Set l to be the root of X;
Choose a literal l ∈L uniformly at random;
else if op = INS then
Choose a node x ∈X uniformly at random;
Choose f ∈F, l ∈L uniformly at random;
Replace x in X with f ;
Set the children of f to be x and l, order chosen uniformly at random;
else if op = DEL then
Choose a leaf node x ∈X uniformly at random;
Replace x’s parent in X with x’s sibling in X;
else if op = SUB then
Choose a node x ∈X uniformly at random
Choose a replacement l ∈L, orf ∈F uniformly at random;
Replace x in X with l if x is a leaf node, or with f if x is an internal node;
end if

the Order, Majority and MAX problems. L is the number of literals. n denotes the maximum number of
nodes, which is equal to 2D+1 −1 for a maximum depth D.

4.2
Theoretical Problems from GP Literature
4
ANALYSIS
Problem 1. (ORDER) F := {J}, L := {x1, x1, . . . , xn, xn} The ﬁtness of a tree X is the
number of literals xi, for which the positive literal xi appears before the negative literal xi in
the in-order parse of X [31]
Problem 2. (MAJORITY) F := {J}, L := {x1, x1, . . . , xn, xn} The ﬁtness of a tree X is
the number of literals xi for which the positive literal xi appears in X at least once, and at
least as many times as the corresponding negative literal xi [31]
Notice that the function set only contains the J operator (short for join) and that it is
irrelevant for the ﬁtness of a tree/model. The upper bounds on the runtime for the (1+1)
GP, RLS-GP and SMO-GP are shown in Table 4.1. The runtime bound for the SMO-GP
contains Tinit, which as Neumann notes in [34] is advantageous compared to Tmax as Tinit is
known before running the algorithm as it just depends on the size of the initial tree.
4.2.2
The MAX Problem
The goal in the MAX problem is to construct a program that gives the highest output
value possible for a given function set F, terminal set T and constraint on the maximum
depth D [28]. Following K¨otzing et al. [25] we denote for ﬁxed D, F, T the Max problem
as MAX-depth-D-F-T. We will in this project consider the variant with F = {+, ×} and
T = {0.5}. The optimal solution is a complete binary tree with multiplication in the root
node, multiplication or addition at the next layer and then addition for all the remaining
layers (see Figure 4.4 for an example with D = 4). The runtime bound for the (1+1) GP
and RLS-GP are listed in Table 4.1.

==PAGE:35==
5
DESIGN
5
Design
In this section we consider the design choices required to later carry out the experimental
part of the analysis of the QLattice and comparison with genetic programming. Initially we
give an overview of the design and structure of the software modules developed as part of
the project. Following this we discuss what benchmarks we are going to run and we perform
an initial analysis of the PMLB data set suite as preparation for the evaluation in Section
7.3 by selecting a subset of data sets to work with.
5.1
Software Design
The software developed for the project consists of three main modules, each of which we
describe brieﬂy below. The modules are loosely coupled and separated in three folders in
the source code for better overview.
Custom Genetic Programming Framework
The custom genetic programming frame-
work developed in this project includes the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP, SMO-GP algorithms (see
Section 4.2) and a symbolic regression algorithm for regression and binary classiﬁcation.
Going forward we will refer to this symbolic regression algorithm as Custom GP for the sake
of brevity. The framework has also been made ﬂexible and extensible so more operators and
algorithm variants can be implemented in the future.
The main purpose of the module is to allow for more speciﬁc kind of benchmarks and
experiments than GPLearn oﬀers through their interface without having to rework a vast
proportion of the code base. In addition to this, having implemented a genetic program-
ming framework from the ground up has also given a more profound understanding of the
mechanisms and methods in genetic programming. This pays dividends when comparing the
QLattice with genetic programming and also makes working with other genetic programming
frameworks such as GPLearn easier.
Benchmarks and Analysis Pipelines
The software for the benchmarks is split into a
number of ﬁles and subfolders grouped by type of test and functionality. This includes utility
ﬁles with conﬁgurations for the tests and parameters for the algorithms, code for generating
plots and wrapper classes for extending functionality of the QLattice and GPLearn algo-
rithms and to integrate it into a streamlined analysis pipeline. The term analysis pipeline is
loosely used here to reﬂect that the tests are set up so they run end-to-end without manual
intervention. This includes the extraction of the data, run of the algorithms, recording of
metrics during the run and saving of the results to a ﬁle.
Visualisation Tool
In order to have more opportunities for visualising and exploring the
data and performance of the algorithms interactively in addition to the benchmarks and
tests shown in this report, a basic dashboard web application has been made. The tool
allows a user to run the QLattice, GPLearn and Custom GP with a chosen conﬁguration
and data set and the dashboard will update and display relevant ﬁgures and metrics from
the run of the algorithm. The dashboard consists of a home page and three subpages:
• The home page gives a quick introduction and overview of the application.
• The ﬁrst subpage includes a form for specifying whether to use PMLB data sets or
synthetic data and for choosing which speciﬁc test case to run. Relevant information
about a data set is also displayed below.
• The second subpage contains a form for choosing the algorithm to run and for choosing
the values of a number of hyper-parameters. Only a subset of the most interesting
27
==PAGE:36==
5.2
Experiments
5
DESIGN
hyper-parameters are available as it would be cumbersome to include them all and be-
cause the application is not meant for as extensive testing as the dedicated benchmarks
written as part of the project.
• The third subpage includes plots that are updated dynamically during training. The
plots include a view of the current best model on the training data, the train and test
scores, mean and best model size and a plot of the Pareto front. The Pareto front plot
shows the Pareto frontier of graphs in the population with respect to the objectives
loss and model size.
For more instructions on usage and how to run the visualisation tool, refer to the README
ﬁle included along side the source code for the project or the Git repository.
5.2
Experiments
We will split up the experiments in four parts, each of which approach the analysis of the
QLattice and comparison with genetic programming in a diﬀerent way. We will ﬁrst run
a benchmark on the three theoretical problems from GP literature, which will serve as a
proof-of-concept that the genetic programming algorithms are able to evolve certain kinds of
structures based on a ﬁtness measure and running the QLattice on these problems will test
to what extent it can be made to run on such problems. After this we will run benchmarks
for synthetic data sets, which will provide some preliminary insight into the performance of
the diﬀerent algorithms. For the synthetic data sets we have full control over the underlying
structure of the data, the level of noise and the diﬃculty of the problems, which will make
it easier to reason about how good the best models found by each algorithms are.
We
then perform benchmarks on real world data sets from the PMLB data set suite where we
focus and address a speciﬁc aspect or property such as investigating the initialisation of the
algorithms, the use of a diversity promoting technique, restricting the search space etc. In
each test we will then study how the predictive performance, model complexity and similar
is aﬀected by the problem and relevant settings and hyper-parameters. Finally at the end
we will make a larger performance comparison test between the QLattice, GPLearn and
Eureqa akin to the test of [38].
By performing the analysis based on both theoretical problems, synthetic data sets and
real world data sets we will have diverse set of tests, which will give diﬀerent kinds of insight.
The majority of benchmarks will be run on data sets from the PMLB data set suite as this
includes real world data sets, which we expect to most closely resemble the kind of data that
the algorithms would normally be used for modelling. By initially making multiple conﬁned
tests for the PMLB data sets in Section 7.3 with a clear focus and scope it will make it
easier to interpret and evaluate the results. Furthermore, running multiple smaller tests
that involve only a subset of all the hyper-parameters will be much faster to run than fewer
larger tests with very large grids of combinations of hyper-parameters to test. At the time
we carry out the ﬁnal performance comparison test between the algorithms in Section 7.4,
we will have a good idea of what hyper-parameters work well and can therefore also choose
a smaller number of hyper-parameter values to test than we would have done with no prior
knowledge. A drawback of this approach is that for the tests involving hyper-parameters, we
might miss out on some very speciﬁc combination of all the hyper-parameters and settings
that would work better than what we try with.
For instance if we test diﬀerent values
for a hyper-parameter such as parsimony pressure separately from testing settings of the
probability of doing crossover in genetic programming we might not discover that a speciﬁc
setting of these two parameters will work much better in terms of predictive performance
than keeping one of them at the default value while changing the other. However, this is
an inherent part of empirical experiments and a balance between the power and certainty
in the results and practicality of setting up and running the experiments has to be struck.
28
==PAGE:37==
5.2
Experiments
5
DESIGN
Where it is relevant we strive for choosing test setups with combinations of settings and
hyper-parameters that we expect might inﬂuence one another with Section 2 in mind.
Another concern when running multiple tests is that we might get a seemingly signiﬁcant
diﬀerence in performance between two parameter settings by the random chance associated
with the stochastic nature of the experiments.
The way we deal with this is by taking
the variance in the average scores achieved for the diﬀerent data sets into account when
comparing the results. We will also keep the comparison between diﬀerent settings slightly
less formal by not making claims of statistical signiﬁcance.
5.2.1
Selection of PMLB Data Set Subsets
We will now delimit the PMLB data sets by choosing a representative subset of data sets.
The reason is that using all 286 PMLB data sets [42] for all benchmarks will be too compu-
tationally expensive. On the other hand, if we work with a subset of data sets the results
could get biased if the data sets chosen are not diﬀerent and diverse enough. We there-
fore now make an exploratory analysis of the PMLB data sets with respect to their meta
features i.e. the number of observations, number of features, class imbalance etc. Based
on this we will identify a representative subset of data sets with diﬀerent characteristics.
