Apart from speeding up testing,
this approach also has the advan-
tage of allowing for using two dif-
ferent subsets of data sets; one we
will use for testing properties and
hyper-parameter settings for the
algorithms (Section 7.3) and one
subset for comparing the perfor-
mance of the QLattice, GPLearn
and Eureqa (Section 7.4).
This
way we avoid some of the bias
that we would otherwise have if we
were to use the same data sets for
both investigating the best hyper-
parameter settings for the QLat-
tice and GPLearn and for compar-
ing the performance with Eureqa.
We will strive for 20 data sets for
regression and 10 for classiﬁcation. The reason for this choice is that we are generally slightly
more interested in regression problems as a regression model is more general in the sense
that it can be translated to a binary classiﬁer.
Figure 5.1 and 5.2 show an overview of the data sets with respect to a number of features.
We see that most of the data sets have a relatively small amount of features and medium
number of observations. We also see the presence of a few data sets with either a high
number of features or observations.
Initially at the start of the work with this project, the QLattice had a constraint of a
maximum number of 200 features, so when selecting data sets to work with we disregarded
the data sets with more than 200 features. Later the maximum number of features was
increased to 2000 features with feyn version 1.5.3 8. Nevertheless, we will still work with
the original limit and only use data sets with at most 200 features in this project. In practice
it is arguably also more common to work with data sets with less than 200 features and most
of the PMLB data sets have below 200 features, so this is not deemed a concern.
To select a representative subset of the data sets we perform a clustering based on the
meta features. Like Olson et al. [37] we use K-means clustering and the silhouette score for

determining a good number of clusters. We perform a clustering on the regression and the

can select a certain number of each while still making the choice for each representative.
Figure 5.3 shows the silhouette scores for the diﬀerent number of clusters for regression (left)
and classiﬁcation (right). We are interested in a relatively low number of clusters since two
many clusters could put too much emphasis on outliers. We therefore choose four clusters
for classiﬁcation. For regression two clusters give the highest silhouette score, but looking
at the silhouette plot in Figure 5.4 (a) we see that one cluster is much bigger than the other
and the average silhouette scores is below average for the smaller of the two. We therefore
decide to use 6 clusters instead for the regression data sets which gives a better division
as seen in Figure 5.3. We now compute the fraction of data sets each cluster makes up of
the total number and use this to compute the number of data sets we should choose from
each cluster to get 20 regression and 10 classiﬁcations data sets. We choose the data sets
uniformly at random from each cluster and get the data sets shown in Table 5.1. These
data sets will be used for the analysis of diﬀerent hyper-parameter settings in Section 7.3.
We then do this again without choosing any of the data sets in Table 5.1 so we also get
20 regression and 10 classiﬁcation data sets for the algorithm performance comparison in
Section 7.4.
Note that the regression data sets 1201 BNG breastTumor, 1196 BNG pharynx and 1203 BNG pwLinear
are struck through in Table 5.1 and Table 5.2. The reason for this is that it became apparent
when running the benchmarks in Section 7.3 that the time required to ﬁt the algorithms
on these very large data sets was too high to be practical. The choice was therefore made
to exclude these even though they were initially chosen. This is not deemed a signiﬁcant
issue as the rest of the chosen data sets should still be representative with respect to the
meta features and there are still data sets with a relatively high number of observations
among those used. The data set The data set analcatdata bankruptcy in Table 5.2 is also

set: A closer inspection reveals that all the response values are either -1 or 1 corresponding
to dying and living, respectively. We will keep this data set going forward but remark that
it is better suited as a classiﬁcation data set.
5.2.2
Test Setup
We now consider how to set up the tests in more detail. We will use one type of evaluation
loop when investigating the eﬀect of diﬀerent parameter settings in Section 7.3 and another
for the benchmark where we compare the performance of the algorithms in Section 7.4.
Algorithm Setting Tests
When comparing hyper-parameter settings for an algorithm we
will use one-layer K-fold cross-validation, where the algorithm with each setting is evaluated
on the same K-fold data splits. We set K = 5, which from preliminary testing seemed to
strike a good balance between lowering variance in the mean test score estimates and the
time it takes to run a test on a data set. Figure 5.5 (left) shows a schematic of the setup.

Figure 5.5: Left: One-layer test approach shown for a single data set. Right: Two-layer test approach for a
data set. The outer layer is repeated M times to compute the average test error for the given data set.
Algorithm Performance Comparison Test
For the performance comparison between
the QLattice, GPLearn and Eureqa we use a two layer approach. The outer layer will be
Monte-Carlo cross-validation and the inner layer will be K-fold-cross-validation (see Figure
5.5 (right)). This way of comparing the performance of the algorithms is the same as in [38].
We use 3 repetitions of the outer layer and 2 folds for the inner layer. These values are
relatively small, but have been chosen as such as it became apparent from initial testing
that the Datarobot Python API for using Eureqa in the free trial has limits to the number of
times new test cases can be run (for more detail refer to Section 6 and 8). Using two layers
for the evaluation loop we can use a parameter grid for the inner layer for each algorithm
of diﬀerent sizes and still get a fair comparison. For instance if we only used one layer and
a parameter grid of 1000 conﬁgurations for one algorithm and another parameter grid of
only a couple of conﬁgurations for another type algorithm, the former algorithm might get
a higher chance of achieving good results by virtue of being stochastic and evaluated more
times.
33
==PAGE:42==
6
IMPLEMENTATION
6
Implementation
In this subsection we describe the implementation of a selected subset of the elements of the
application in more detail. We limit ourselves to describing the visualisation tool, Custom
GP, how the QLattice is run on the theoretical problems from Section 4.2, the genetic marker
method by [10] applied to the QLattice and use of the Datarobot Python API for comparing
the QLattice and GPLearn with Eureqa in Section 7.4.
6.1
Visualisation Tool
The visualisation tool consists of a frontend made with Dash9, a Python backend and a
redis10 database. The backend exposes three RESTful API endpoints for the frontend using
Flask11 for starting, pausing and resetting training of an algorithm. The redis database
is used for sharing settings chosen in the frontend and for sharing the results and metrics
generated in the backend while an algorithm is running on a chosen test case. When running
the production build, the frontend is served in a Docker container using uWSGI12 and
Nginx13 based on a preconﬁgured docker image from Docker Hub14.
When running in
development mode the default webserver of Dash is used (a Flask based development web
server) for serving the frontend.
6.2
Custom Genetic Programming Framework
The custom genetic programming framework has been written in Python and represents a
graph with a simple explicit tree based structure with each node being an class instance
with a label, function, parent reference and a list of subtree references. For leaf nodes the
function is set to None and for internal nodes the label is set to the empty string. The
functions are represented as a collections.namedtuple of an arity, string formatter, name
and callback function for evaluating the node.
6.3
Theoretical Problems
The QLattice is able to be used with the theoretical problems from GP (see Section 4.2 and
7.1) by deﬁning a custom sorting function for each problem, which the QGraph head is sorted
by after a call to feyn. qgraph.fit(). The custom sorting functions sort the graphs in
descending order with respect to ﬁtness, which is assigned each graph at the start of every
iteration. The ﬁtness of a graph is computed by traversing the graph using the dictionary
representation of the graph from a call to feyn. graph. to dict().
As an example, we consider the implementation of the Majority problem here. The
code listing below shows the custom sorting function. For each graph the feature names are
extracted using the graph dictionary. Using a defaultdict(int) instance the count of each
literal is counted after which the ﬁtness is computed by summing the number of positive
literals with a higher count than their corresponding negative literal.

6.4
Genetic Markers Applied to the QLattice
The genetic marker approach described in section 2.2.4 is implemented and applied for
the QLattice by treating each interaction in the graph as if it were a node in a genetic
programming tree. The root node is perceived as the last function interaction (the target
node is not considered root).
Only the type of function or feature and the input and
output edges are considered meaning weight terms and locations are disregarded.
Like
for the theoretical problem the genetic marker of a graph is computed using the dictionary
representation of a graph. Figure 6.1 shows the genetic marker for an example graph starting
at the root and with a maximum depth of 1.

6.5
Datarobot Python API
The Datarobot Python API was used instead of the graphical user interface for integrating
Eureqa into the analysis pipeline. This way the test could be run without need for manual
intervention and to ensure that the algorithms are evaluated on the same train and test
splits. One challenge with using the API, however, is that it does not seem readily apparent
how to get the train error after training completes for a given train-test-split. Normally the
train error is not so usable as it is a biased estimate of the generalisation error, but in this
case we are interested in knowing both the train and test error in order to determine how
well the model generalises. To get the train error one option is to upload the train data as
a so-called predictions data set. This has the drawback of slowing down training, however,
and it turned out from initial testing during development that it leads to a 429 server code
error when too many predictions sets are uploaded in too short a timespan after which a very
long cool down period is imposed. To circumvent this, we exploit the train-validation-test
split partitioning scheme, by setting the train, validation and test data for Eureqa to be
the train data, test data and a copy of the train data, respectively. We can then get the
test error as the validation error from the API and the train error as the error computed
on the held-out set. We can do this since the validation error is only used by Datarobot
for comparing multiple diﬀerent types of models and since we only train one type of model,
namely Eureqa with the default parameters and number of iterations of training, the test
error estimate will not be biased. The code listing below shows the approach:
1
df_train[’partition_splits ’] = [0 for _ in range(df_train.shape [0])]
2
df_test[’partition_splits ’] = [1 for _ in range(df_test.shape [0])]
3
df_train_holdout [’partition_splits ’] = [2 for _ in range( df_train_holdout .shape [0])]
4
5
df_combined = pd.concat ([ df_train , df_test , df_train_holdout ])
6

partitioning_method = partitioning_method )

machine learning training process involving multiple types of models. The so-called blueprint
is speciﬁed to be Eureqa with a default number of iterations of training, since we are only
interested in training one Eureqa model. Next, the training is started as a blocking asyn-

==PAGE:46==
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
7
Evaluation and Results
We will now carry out the experiments and evaluation of the QLattice and genetic pro-
gramming algorithms.
We start with evaluating the QLattice on the theoretical genetic
programming problems in Section 7.1 and then set up and run benchmarks for synthetic
test cases in Section 7.2. Next we run tests of diﬀerent settings of hyper-parameters and
methods on the PMLB data sets in Section 7.3 and ﬁnally in Section 7.4 we carry out the
comparison of the algorithms.
We use feyn version 1.5.3, gplearn version 0.4.1 and datarobot version 2.24.0. The
software running the QLattice component of the QLattice algorithm has been updated a
couple of times during the project period, but the vast majority of the ﬁnal test results have
been performed with the same production build that was live during April. Unless otherwise
stated we use the default set of parameters for the QLattice and GPLearn from said Python
module versions with the exception that we for GPLearn use the entire function set available
instead of only addition, subtraction, multiplication and division (The full function set of
GPLearn is listed in appendix A. Preliminary testing during work with the project suggested
that GPLearn performed signiﬁcantly better with a larger function set, so the choice was
made early on to use all functions by default. In Section 7.3.4 we also make a test that
showcases this diﬀerence. For the parameters of the Custom GP refer to the source code.
Finally, note that when listing the model size of the QLattice we use the number of edges as
a model complexity measure and for GPLearn and Custom GP we use the number of nodes.
7.1
Theoretical Problems
We evaluate the QLattice and compare with the algorithms (1+1) GP, RLS-GP and SMO-
GP (see Section 4.2) for which we know upper bounds on the runtime given in Table 4.1. We
ﬁrst evaluate the algorithms on the Order and Majority problems and then the MAX
problem. For the QLattice we run the normal ﬁtting loop (see Figure 4.1), but sort the
QGraph head between ﬁtting loop step 1 and 2 in descending order based on the ﬁtness of
each graph evaluated with respect to the theoretical problem (refer to Section 6.3 for more
details for running the QLattice on the Majority problem). The goal with these tests will
be to compare the genetic programming algorithms with the theoretical runtime bounds and
to investigate how the QLattice behaves for these kind of problems, which are very diﬀerent
from what the QLattice is normally used for.
When evaluating the QLattice we will run it in two conﬁgurations, which we will refer
to as conﬁguration (a) and conﬁguration (b):
(a) Standard conﬁguration without any changes to the QLattice algorithm
(b) Adapted conﬁguration where duplicate graphs are not removed, graph decay and ex-
tinction events are disabled and more than 50 graphs are allowed to have the same x-
lattice location. The change is achieved by commenting out the call to feyn. qlattice. algo x
in feyn. qlattice.fit in the feyn python module source code.
The reason for this is that discarding graphs can have adverse eﬀects on the optimisation
times for some of these theoretical problems. Moreover it is interesting to see how the two
conﬁgurations compare which will give more insight into the inner workings of the QLattice.
7.1.1
Order and Majority
GP on Order
We start by running the (1+1) GP, (1+1) GP*, RLS-GP, RLS-GP* and
SMO-GP on the Order problem for a diﬀerent number of positive literals and compute the
number of iterations it takes each algorithm to ﬁnd the optimal solution (the optimisation
time). For each number of positive literals L we run the algorithms 10 times starting with
38
==PAGE:47==
7.1
Theoretical Problems
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
an initial tree with a single random terminal and compute the average optimisation time.
We also impose a maximum depth limit of 25 on the trees generated, though this depth is
set so high that it should never be reached. Figure 7.1 shows the results. We immediately

Figure 7.1: Genetic programming algorithms on the Order problem. Left: The optimisation time. Right:
The model size (number of nodes). Averaged over 10 runs. For each algorithm the standard error of the
mean is shown in transparent colour. Note that the plot of the model size for SMO-GP is slightly obscured
by the plot for RLS-GP*, since they are very similar.
observe that the optimisation time is considerably higher for SMO-GP compared to the
(1+1) GP an RLS-GP for high L. Looking at the runtime bounds in Table 4.1 we might
not have expected such a big diﬀerence as the upper bound for RLS-GP* is only a factor
log L smaller than that of SMO-GP. The diﬀerence could be explained by the upper bound
for RLS-GP* not being tight, which is line with a study by Urli et al. [55] who conjecture
that the upper bound on the optimisation time for RLS-GP* is O(Tinit + L log L). The
(1+1) GP* exhibits greater variance in optimisation time than the (1+1) GP, which may
be explained by the the (1+1) GP* getting stuck at a suboptimal solution where it requires
multiple HVL-Prime operations to get to a solution with strictly better ﬁtness.
Looking at the increase in model size of the best solution it seems to increase linearly
with L. This makes good sense as the minimum number of leaves in the optimal solution
increases linearly with L and therefore also the number of nodes in the trees.
We also
see that the SMO-GP is able to ﬁnd smaller solutions than the (1+1) GP and RLS-GP,
which may be explained by the the SMO-GP optimising for both ﬁtness and model size.
Smaller and simpler models are generally desired, so this can be seen as an advantage of
the SMO-GP. However, as the results suggest, this comes at the expense of an increase in
optimisation time. Interesting to observe is also that for this problem RLS-GP* like the
SMO-GP consistently ﬁnds the smallest solutions among the algorithms. This makes sense
as the act of only accepting oﬀspring with strictly better ﬁtness while at the same time
only making a single HVL-Prime mutation operation per iteration means that RLS-GP*
will never accept oﬀspring that introduce negative literals and the model size will therefore
generally be low.
GP on Majority
Next we run the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP and SMO-GP on the Majority
problem in the same manner as for the Order problem. Figure 7.2 shows the result. The
results appear similar to those for the Order problem with what looks like an almost linear
increase in optimisation time for the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP, (1+1) GP* and RLS-GP*. This
is in line with Table 4.1 from which we expect an asymptotically almost linear upper bound
on the optimisation time for the (1+1) GP and RLS-GP. We again see that the SMO-GP
has a higher optimisation time and that it appears to increase more rapidly. This is also
expected as the upper bound includes L2.

on the Order and Majority problems in a similar manner as the genetic programming

not found within a practical number of iterations for higher values or a timeout server error
occurred. It is clear from the results that the QLattice algorithm is not tailored towards
solving these two theoretical problems as the algorithm was only able to ﬁnd the optimum
for a low number of positive literals within a feasible amount of time. This is not a surprising
result, however, considering the way the QLattice works: The QLattice does not generate
new graphs using evolutionary operators that directly modify the tree structure like the
40
==PAGE:49==
7.1
Theoretical Problems
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
HVL-Prime mutation operator does; instead better graphs will have to be generated by
sampling the right interactions from the probability distribution of the QLattice and if the
best graphs used for updating the probability distribution are very similar, the search will
quickly converge to a suboptimal solution. Moreover, the graphs produced by the QLattice
are not necessarily trees as an interaction can have an output to multiple nodes. This can
have an adverse eﬀect on the promotion of many leaf nodes and for Majority in particular
this seems to make it hard to get a high ﬁtness.
7.1.2
The MAX Problem
Finally we consider the MAX problem, where we run benchmarks for variant MAX-depth-
D-{+, ×}-{0.5}, where the function set consists of addition and multiplication and the
terminal set of the constant 0.5. We run the algorithms for diﬀerent values of the maximum
depth D and compute the optimisation time.
GP on the MAX Problem
Figure 7.5 shows the optimisation time for the (1+1) GP,
RLS-GP and SMO-GP averaged over 10 runs. For this benchmark we do not plot the model
size as it is the same for all the algorithms for a given max depth as the optimal solution
tree always has the same size equal to 2D+1 −1 nodes. From Table 4.1 we would expect the
RLS-GP to be slightly faster asymptotically than the (1+1) GP and this is in line with what
we observe with the SMO-GP using the most iterations followed by the (1+1) GP and then
RLS-GP. We also see that the optimisation time increases rapidly for all three algorithms as
a function of D. This is also as expected as the number of nodes n in the optimal solution
increases exponentially with D.

QLattice on the MAX Problem
Figure 7.6 shows the result for the QLattice, which
was run 5 times for every value of D in conﬁguration (a) and (b). Some runs for the QLattice
in conﬁguration (a) did not ﬁnd the optimum within a practical number of iterations (in this
test set to 75) for D > 4, so the optimisation time for conﬁguration (a) is only shown up to
D = 4 in the plot. We see that conﬁg (b) was able to more consistently solve the problem
for greater maximum depths but the variance in optimisation time is high compared to that
of the genetic programming algorithms.
An interesting observation is that the model size is not the same between runs of the
algorithm for the same maximum depth as seen in the right plot of Figure 7.6, which is
contrary to the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP and SMO-GP. This diﬀerence is caused by the graphs
for the QLattice sometimes using the output of previous interactions. An example oﬀthis
is seen by the graph in Figure 7.7, which shows the best graph found for D = 4 during one

To gain more insight into how the two conﬁgurations of the QLattice diﬀer, we plot the
ﬁtness as a function of iterations for the ﬁve runs of conﬁguration (a) and (b) for the MAX
problem with D = 4 (see Figure 7.8). From this plot we see that the ﬁtness for the runs using
QLattice conﬁguration (a) ﬂuctuates while it never decreases for conﬁguration (b). This
makes sense as conﬁguration (b) never discards the graph with the highest ﬁtness, which
might happen for conﬁguration (a) as ﬁtness is not considered in feyn. qgraph. algo x;
loss is considered but has no meaning in this experiment. For the MAX problem we can see
that not discarding graphs is a clear advantage in terms of optimisation time. It is important
to emphasise, however, that it comes with the drawback that the memory consumption and
CPU time spent on the client side running the benchmark for conﬁguration (b) increases
with each iteration as more and more graphs are kept and sorted in the QGraph head.

7.2
Synthetic Data Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
7.2
Synthetic Data Tests
We now evaluate the QLattice on regression and classiﬁcation problems based on synthetic
data and compare the performance with GPLearn and the Custom GP implementation. We
start by describing the set up for the tests and make a preliminary analysis of the CPU time
used by the algorithms in order to choose a fair number of iterations to run each algorithm.
We then analyse the performance on the synthetic data sets with and without a noise term
added to the response values.
Data Set Construction
We construct the test cases by choosing a number of mathemat-
ical formulas with predictors x0, x1, . . . and response y. We then select a number of evenly
spaced values for each predictor in a certain range and compute the response based on the
mathematical formula underlying the test case in question. The combination of predictors
and response when collected as rows in a table gives rise to a data set that is split into a train
and test set to train and test the algorithms, respectively. The data is shuﬄed randomly
before splitting the data. For each regression test case we record the response y with and
without adding a Gaussian noise term with mean 0 and standard deviation 0.5 multiplied
by the standard deviation of the response without noise. For simplicity we only run the
classiﬁcation test cases without noise.
Table 7.1 shows the chosen formulas. The Features column shows the number of features
in the data set. If the number of features is higher than what appears in the formula then
extra features are included in the data set, which do not appear in the formula. These
extra features are thereby superﬂuous and will challenge the algorithms to select only the
features, which have a signal. The threshold speciﬁes how accurate a model needs to be
on the test data to count as having found the right formula. For the regression cases the
threshold is the test R2 score and for the classiﬁcation cases the threshold is the test AUC
score. Note that the best possible test score is therefore always a value of 1. The thresholds
are set slightly below 1 for all the tests where we want the exact formula to allow solutions
with weights slightly oﬀand to avoid issues with ﬂoating point precision. Lastly the xrange
shows the range in which the values for the features x0, x1, . . . are chosen when generating
the response values for the synthetic data.
No.

Features
Threshold
xrange
Task

Table 7.1: Formulas used for the synthetic test cases. Note that log is the natural logarithm.
The formulas have been chosen to have diﬀerent characteristics. The ﬁrst three formulas
1-3 use simple expressions with no extra superﬂuous features, which should provide relatively
easy test cases. Formula 4 is a more complicated formula with a lower threshold so the models
are allowed to ﬁnd an approximative expression as solution instead of a perfect solution.
Formula 5 contains a logarithm within a logarithm, which provides a test case with an inner
function, which does not aﬀect the response by much as the logarithm function increases
slowly in the range [1, 3]. Formula 6 includes weights diﬀerent from 1 in front of two of the
43
==PAGE:52==
7.2
Synthetic Data Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
features in the formula, which will have to be accurate in order to surpass the threshold
of 0.99. Formula 7 has a simple structure but contains many superﬂuous features, which
ideally should not be used. Formula 8 is the sine function, which is not in the function
set of the QLattice and Custom GP and will test the extent to which these algorithms are
able to ﬁnd an approximation for this function. Finally formula 9 and 10 are two simple
classiﬁcation test cases where the model needs to ﬁnd the decision boundary given by the
hyperplane x0 + x1 and x0 · x1 + x0 −0.05, respectively.
Hardware Speciﬁcation
The primary hardware speciﬁcation used for the benchmarks
on the synthetic test cases is as follows: An Intel i5-4690K quad-core 6 MB Cache CPU
and 16 GB 1833MHz DDR3 RAM. The tests are carried out on a system running Manjaro
Linux version 21.0 and kernel 5.10. These are the speciﬁcations for the client side. Recall
from 2.3 that the the QLattice part of the QLattice algorithm runs on a cluster for which
the hardware is unknown.
Wall-Clock Time
Before running the benchmarks on the synthetic test cases we ﬁrst
investigate the diﬀerence in wall-clock time of the algorithms.
Based on this we choose
a number of iterations of training for each algorithm that gives a fair amount of wall-
clock time of training to each algorithm.
To this end, we consider a formula given by
y = x0 + x1 + x2 + x3 + x4 with 5 features and an xrange of [−1, 1]. We run each algorithm
for 10 iterations and record the wall-clock time it takes to ﬁnish training (see Figure 7.9
(left)).
From these results we have that the QLattice spends roughly 5 times as long as GPLearn
on one iteration. We will therefore allot 5 times as many iterations of training for GPLearn
as the QLattice.
The Custom GP is the slowest of the three algorithms, which is not
surprising as it only runs on a single thread and is not optimised much for speed, memory
and cache utilisation.
For a fair comparison in terms of speed the Custom GP should
be run with roughly half as many iterations as the QLattice. Nevertheless, we shall run
the Custom GP algorithm with as many iterations as GPLearn in order to achieve more
competitive results to compare with. If more time would have been invested in optimising
the Custom GP implementation the running time could be lowered signiﬁcantly, which has
not been a priority as already mentioned in Section 6. The Custom GP and GPLearn are
still comparable in terms of predictive performance as they make similar operations in each
iteration.
Since the QLattice algorithm runs both client side (feyn) and on a computing cluster
(QLattice), we make another plot to get an impression of how much of the runtime is spent
client side, which will indicate how important the hardware resources of the client is for
performance of the algorithm. Figure 7.9 (right) shows a plot of the time in seconds spent
on the feyn side and the QLattice cluster side derived from the header information of the
response of the POST request for generating new graphs in ﬁtting loop step 4 of the algorithm
(feyn. qlattice. generate). The time measured for feyn here also includes the traﬃc
time for the HTTPS traﬃc with the QLattice cluster, but this is not deemed signiﬁcant. To
verify this we make a quick inspection of the timing of the packets sent during the run of
the QLattice. From this we see that the time between the client system sending the ﬁrst
SYN packet for the TCP handshake with the server and the time it receives a SYN/ACK is
approximately 0.0228 seconds averaged over three trials. This gives us a rough estimate
of the latency. Since the latency is very low and in each iteration only two POST requests
are made (when generating new graphs at the start of an iteration and when updating the
QLattice with learnings from an iteration) the time spent on HTTPS traﬃc is considered
negligible.
Looking at Figure 7.9, we see that the time spent on the feyn side is relatively consistent
judging by the low standard error on the mean time and that is similar to the time spent
on the cluster. From this result there does not appear to be a critical need for pursuing
44

better hardware for running the synthetic tests, so we will carry on running the synthetic
benchmarks on the system with the hardware listed above.

7.2.1
Without Noise
We ﬁrst run the benchmarks where no noise term is added to the data. The response y for
each test case is therefore equal to the output of the corresponding formula evaluated on
the predictors. We run the QLattice for 10 iterations and train GPLearn and the Custom
GP for 50 iterations and each algorithm is run 5 times in total. We use 100 data points for
each formula with 1/3 of the observations being randomly assigned to the test set. This is a
relatively low number of data points but since there is no noise term it should still be easy
for the algorithms to generalise to the test set.
Success Count
Figure 7.10 shows a bar chart of the number of times each algorithm
achieved a test score equal to or above the threshold in one of the iterations during training
for each test case. We see from the plot that all three algorithms are able to ﬁnd a model
that exceeds the test score threshold for many of the test cases at least once. As expected
case 8 is not solved by the QLattice as it does not include a sine function and therefore
cannot accurately enough surpass the threshold within 10 iterations. The same is the case
for the Custom GP. On the other hand GPLearn cannot solve case case 3, which involves
an exponential function for the same reason, while the QLattice ﬁnds the optimum in 4 of
out the 5 runs. Another interesting observation is that the QLattice is much better at case
6, which involves weights diﬀerent from 1 in front of two features. Because the QLattice
uses stochastic gradient descent to tune weights for every feature in a graph this task is
easy for the QLattice, but for GPLearn and Custom GP the weights have to be chosen as
ephemeral scalars and combined with the right feature in a multiplication operation, which
makes it more challenging. Surprisingly the QLattice does not ﬁnd the optimal solution in
any of the ﬁve runs for case 2, where an optimal solution for the QLattice would be a graph
consisting one add, one inverse and one multiply interaction and the three input features.
We consider this in further detail below.
Test Score As a Function of Iterations
Next we plot for each algorithm and test case
the average, maximum and minimum test scores (R2 score for regression and AUC score for
classiﬁcation) and average, maximum and minimum best model size at every iteration. The
results are shown in Figure B.1 and Figure B.2 in the appendix. The predictive performance
on case 2, 4 and 8 is shown in Figure 7.11 as well for ease of reference. Returning to case 2
we see that the test R2 score for the QLattice starts out very low in the ﬁrst few iterations
and converges to a value between -2.5 and 0.12. The reason the QLattice unlike the genetic
programming based algorithms was unable to ﬁnd the solution in at least one of the runs may

Figure 7.10: Bar chart showing the number of times the threshold was exceeded when running the algorithms

be explained by the xrange being [-1, 1]. This results in many observations with x2 being
close to 0 causing the response to be very high as x0 −x1 is divided by x2 in the formula.
This in turn, can result in a high squared error even when the graph includes an inverse
interaction as the maximum magnitude of the protected division function for the QLattice
in equation (4.2) is only 20. Graphs with other types of interactions might therefore be
preferred instead causing the search to move away from a graph that perfectly captures the
underlying formula. GPLearn and the custom GP use protected division (equation (2.1 with
ε = 0.01) with a maximum magnitude of 100, so the problem is only slightly less prevalent.
Looking at the average best model size we also clearly see that although GPLearn and the
Custom GP solve the test case with high test R2 scores, the mean best model size is also
much higher than the optimal solution of 5 nodes. These results highlight that the numerical
stability of the protected functions come at the expense of adversely overconstraining the
predictions in some cases.
Looking at case 8 with the sine function, we see that despite the QLattice not ﬁnding
the optimum solution the test R2 score is still very high in all 5 runs. Looking at the best
model size we see, that the mean best model size is relatively high, however, with around 14
edges after 10 iterations. On the other hand, for case 4 where the QLattice was able to ﬁnd
a model with test R2 above the threshold of 0.9, the best mean model size for the QLattice
is only around 12 edges. The best model sizes for GPLearn and Custom GP on case 4 are
also low, but the models were not accurate enough to exceed the threshold of 0.9.

Best Model Inspection
Finally we inspect some of the best models found. Figure 7.12
(a) and (b) show the best graphs for the QLattice on case 9 from the ﬁrst run at the ﬁrst
46
==PAGE:55==
7.2
Synthetic Data Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
and last iteration of training. Both graphs classify all observations correctly. Nevertheless,
we clearly see that the model complexity has increased during training and the graph at the
last iteration arguably contains a lot of redundant interactions as the decision boundary can
be described by the hyperplane x0+x1−0.5. The reason more interactions are added during
may be explained by the train loss being binary cross-entropy, which is lowered from 0.015
after the ﬁrst iteration to 7.39 · 10−7 at the last iteration. Despite the model after the ﬁrst
iteration classifying all observations correctly the decision boundary is not as sharp with
greater uncertainty in the classiﬁcation of the observations near the boundary compared to
that of the graph at the last iteration (see Figure 7.12 (c) and (d)). In this case the decision
boundary in subplot (c) is still preferred, however, and the greater uncertainty at the top left
is desirable. This model inspection shows that care must be taken when selecting the best
model and that taking model size into account in addition to train loss can be beneﬁcial.

three models achieve a high test R2 score though as the logarithm function increases slowly
in the range x0 ∈[1, 3]. For instance for graph (a) in the ﬁgure the second log function is
modelled by the linear and tanh functions. Still, it is possible to achieve an R2 train and
test score of 1.0 with only two log applications, so this shows redundancy for graph (b) and
(c) in the ﬁgures.

QLattice graph model indicates the train squared error loss.
7.2.2
With Noise
We now run the benchmark for the algorithms for the 8 regression test cases with a noise term
added to the response values. The noise term is as mentioned earlier Gaussian distributed
with mean 0 and standard deviation 0.5 multiplied by the standard deviation of the output
values without noise. We again run each algorithm ﬁve times for each test case and let 1/3
of the observations for every data set be the test set. We now generate 300 instead of 100
observations in total for every test case, however, to make it easier to generalise from the
train set to the test set.
Test Score As a Function of Iterations
Figure B.3 shows the predictive performance
results and Figure B.4 shows the best model model size. Figure 7.17 shows the predictive
performance for case 3, 4 and 6. Figure 7.18 shows the best model size for case 1, 2 and
3. We immediately see that the test scores are generally lower because of the noise, but
also that the algorithms generally achieve a decent test score on average. Looking at the
predictive performance changes in case 3, 4 and 6 in particular we see that the QLattice
starts out with the highest test score from the ﬁrst iteration and that it only increases
slightly afterwards, whereas the diﬀerence in test score for GPLearn and the Custom GP is
much higher between the ﬁrst and last iteration. This may be explained by the QLattice
using stochastic gradient descent to tune the weights, which will ensure that the best model
after the ﬁrst iteration already gives a good test score, while it will takes some iterations
of training before the genetic programming based algorithms will choose better ephemeral
scalars. On case 2 we see that the average test R2 score for the QLattice starts out at
-0.2025 after the ﬁrst iteration and then drops after 5 iterations with a minimum test R2
score reaching -41.1 in the third run. This may again be explained by the presence of some
high response values far from the predictions of the best model found.
Looking closer at the best model size changes we see that the best model size for GPLearn
gets very high for case 2 and 3 in comparison with the QLattice and Custom GP. For case
2 the best model size increases without the test R2 score increasing signiﬁcantly, so this
is a clear indication of bloat being developed. We also see this for Custom GP for case 2,
but to a much lesser degree and for case 3 the Custom GP ﬁnds the smallest sized best
model among all three algorithms, which is still on a par with the average test R2 score of
the QLattice. For the QLattice we see that the model size increases a bit for case 1 and 6
without much of an increase in test R2 score.

7.2.3
Bias-Variance Decomposition
The extended bias-variance-decomposition presented in Section 2.4.3 was applied in the
context of genetic programming by Owen et al.
[39], but it is also applicable for other
stochastic evolutionary methods. It is therefore interesting to apply to the QLattice to get
a sense of how useful such an analysis is for understanding the trade-oﬀbetween bias and
variance for the QLattice as well as how the results compare with genetic programming as
in the paper.
We use the same mathematical formula for generating the synthetic data samples:
h(x) = 2.3(x −a) + sin
 2π(x −a)2
(7.1)
t = h(x) + Gaussian(0, 0.3)
(7.2)
where a = 1 and x ∈U[1, 2[ (x values sampled uniformly at random from the half open
interval from 1 to 2). The Gaussian distribution is with mean 0 and standard deviation 0.3.
We will benchmark the QLattice with three diﬀerent function sets that oﬀer diﬀerent levels
of ﬂexibility on the graphs that can be produced:
• Function set 1: All functions in the QLattice function set (see Table ??)
• Function set 2: add, multiply and linear
• Function set 3: add, multiply
As outlined in Section 2.4.3 we randomly generate M samples of N data points based on
the formula above. We then run the QLattice R times with a diﬀerent random seed on
each of the M samples with 15 iterations of training in each run. We do this four times
in total for four diﬀerent max depth restrictions imposed on the QLattice: 1, 2, 3 and 4.
Following Owen et al. [39] we set M = 10, R = 10, N = 1000 in this test. Using equations
(2.26), (2.27) and (2.28) we then compute the bias, external variance and internal variance,
respectively each iteration of training. In [39] the authors use Tukey’s method to identify
and remove outlier models that give extreme predictions prior to computing the bias and
variance. The QLattice generally seems more stable in its predictions than some genetic
programming algorithms such as GPLearn and Custom GP, which will also become more
apparent in Section 7.3), so we therefore skip this step.
Figure 7.21 shows the results. Each row of subplots pertains the results of one function
set and each subplot shows a heat map of the bias, external variance or internal variance
for every iteration during training for every max depth restriction imposed on the QLattice
graphs. The colour bars at the bottom show the heat map values for each of the subplots in
the corresponding column. We see that the bias is the highest for function set 3 for all max
depths as well as for function set 2 with a max depth constraint of 1. This is as expected
as function set 3 does not have the suﬃciency in the function set to properly capture the
structure of the data. With a higher max depth the bias decreases for function set 2 and 1,
51
==PAGE:60==
7.2
Synthetic Data Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
which may be explained by the graphs being allowed to be more complex. Interestingly the
bias is the same for function set 1 and 2 for a max depth of 3 and 4 after some iterations of
training, which suggests that function set 2 is enough to properly model the data. Looking
at the internal variance we see that it starts out high and then decreases and ends up being
around the same at the end of training for max depth 4 for all three function sets. This
result suggests that the QLattice with a larger function set does not exhibit higher variance
and is stable in ﬁnding a high performing model. In [39] the internal variance for the genetic
programming algorithm for all three function increases a small amount from the ﬁrst few
iterations and then remains relatively stable at around 0.05. The internal variance is thereby
lower for the QLattice compared to the genetic programming models in the paper. Finally,
we consider the external variance, which we see is very low for all three function sets and
all max depths and iterations as the scale of the colour bar is very low.
This suggests
that the variance of the QLattice models are not aﬀected much by the randomness in the
data sampling. In [39] two of the three function sets evaluated also receive a uniformly low
external variance for a = 1 and x ∈[1, 2[. It is not surprising that the external variance is
low for this problem as it is a synthetic data set with a relatively high number observations
N. On a real world data set such as PMLB we would expect the contribution of the external
variance to the total error to be much higher.
From the plots we also are able to determine what appears to be the best model conﬁg-
uration. Since the bias is the same for a max depth of 3 and 4 and the internal variance is
lowest for a max depth of 3 for function set 2 it seems that for this problem function set 2
with a maximum depth of 3 given iterations of training allotted is the best choice.

7.3

7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
We now run benchmarks for the QLattice and GPLearn on the subset of PMLB data sets
listed in Table 5.1. As outlined in Section 5.2 the goal is to investigate diﬀerent aspects and
properties of the algorithms and how diﬀerent parameter settings aﬀect the performance. In
this section, when comparing the QLattice with GPLearn we focus on comparing how each
algorithm is aﬀected by diﬀerent settings and work with diﬀerent methods. In section 7.4
we will focus more on comparing the performance of the algorithms. We organise the ex-
periments by grouping benchmarks that pertain to the same aspect in separate subsections.
In each subsection we will motivate and state the aim of the tests to carry out and then
present and discuss the results.
Main Test Setup
We evaluate the algorithms using one-layer cross-validation as de-
scribed in Section 5.2, where we use 5 folds. We will run the QLattice for 25 iterations and
GPLearn for 50 iterations unless otherwise speciﬁed. These default number of iterations
have been chosen to strike a reasonable balance between the time it takes to run a bench-
mark and long enough training to properly reﬂect how the algorithms will perform if they
were to be applied in a real setting.
Visualisation of the Results
For each benchmark we will generate line charts for the
predictive performance and model complexity at each iteration of training for all the data
sets evaluated on. Each point in the plots will be the average score and the standard error
of the mean will be shown for each point with transparent colour. This will allow us to get
a ﬁne grained view of the performance and how it changes during training. For selected
benchmarks we will also visualise the results using a box plot of the test scores computed
based on the test scores of the best models at the end of training in each of the 5 cross-
validation folds.
To make it easier to compare the diﬀerent settings and make conclusions we also generate
a summary table for each benchmark, which we refer to as the ranking scores. The summary
table will contain for each algorithm conﬁguration the mean test score, mean best model
size, a weighted score and the number of times the highest test score at the end of training
for each data set was achieved among the conﬁgurations. When computing the summary
scores we set mean negative scores for data sets to 0. Note that this will only aﬀect the
results for the regression cases as the AUC score for classiﬁcation will always be between 0
and 1. We do this as we want to perceive all negative test R2 scores for the regression test
cases as equally poor and avoid a very large negative test scores on a single data set aﬀecting
the mean too much. The mean test score and mean best model size will be computed by
aggregating across all the data sets. We compute the weighted score as in [56] by, for each
data set, awarding the conﬁguration with the highest test score (where we also set it to 0 if
it is negative) at the end of training C points, the next best C −1 points and so on where
C is the number of conﬁgurations. The ﬁnal score for a conﬁguration is then the sum of the
corresponding weighted scores. When two conﬁgurations achieve the same test score they
each get assigned the same weighted score and if they both tie on the best test score they
will both be awarded a ﬁrst place increment. Note that the sum of ﬁrst place scores of the
algorithms can therefore be higher than the number of data sets evaluated on. Two or more
conﬁgurations tie on the same rank if the relative tolerance diﬀerence in is test score is less
than or equal to 0.001 (using math.isclose with rel tol set to 0.001).
7.3.1
Preprocessing
It is common practice when using many supervised machine learning algorithms to prepro-
cess the data before ﬁtting the model. Linear regression for instance will generally perform
better when the features are not on vastly diﬀerent scales, so it is advised to initially stan-
54
==PAGE:63==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
dardise the features [19]. Many machine learning algorithms also expect no missing data
points or a speciﬁc kind encoding of the categorical features.
In this project preprocessing does not play a big role, however. First of all, the PMLB
data sets has already been preprocessed such that there are no missing data points and
the categorical features are ordinal encoded [37]. The QLattice and GPLearn can therefore
readily be trained on the data without a need of handling these steps ourselves. Further-
more, it is not required by the user to standardise the data before training the QLattice to
get good performance as this is handled by the algorithm by the scale term associated with
each numerical input feature [1]. Traditionally standardisation is not applied for genetic
programming either [39]. Nevertheless, it is interesting to see to what extent such prepro-
cessing steps aﬀect the performance of the QLattice and GPLearn algorithms. We therefore
run a benchmark with four common ways of preprocessing the data prior to training the
algorithms depending on whether or not standardisation and one-hot encoding of the cate-
gorical features are carried out. We will only test the diﬀerence in standardisation on the
regression data sets as the only two data sets in Table 5.1 that have categorical variables
are not used because they contain too many observations. Below we brieﬂy summarise how
we perform standardisation and one-hot-encoding.
Standardisation and One-Hot Encoding
We use z-score standardisation, where the
input features are transformed to have mean 0 and unit variance: [19, 39]
˜X =


where
ˆµj = 1
N

Xij,

i=1(Xij −µj)2
(7.4)
We apply one-hot-encoding also known as 1-out-of-K encoding by transforming each cat-
egorical feature A to K binary feature vectors such that each new feature vector denotes
the presence of a particular value of the categorical feature x e.g. if the given data set
has observations with either the value 0, 1 and 2 for feature A then the new feature vector
that corresponds to the value 0 will contain a one for the observations that had value 0 for
feature A; the other feature vectors will have a 0 entry. The idea behind one-hot-encoding
is to make the ordering of the ordinal value of each categorical value irrelevant, which can
be advantageous in some cases. A drawback is that more features are introduced, however.
We apply standardisation and one-hot encoding using the StandardScaler and OneHotEncoder
classes from the preprocessing submodule of sklearn. To ensure that no information from
the test set is leaked, we ﬁt the StandardScaler and OneHotEncoder instances on the train
data only and transform the train and test data based on this. We identify the categorical
features as the features in a data set of type categorical and binary in the meta information
ﬁles associated with each data set available as part of the pmlb Python module.
Results
Table 7.24 and 7.25 show the ranking score tables for the QLattice on the regres-
sion and classiﬁcation data sets. We see that there does not appear to be much diﬀerence in
predictive performance between standardisation and no standardisation as expected. One-
hot encoding does not appear to make much of a diﬀerence either, which is somewhat
surprising. The reason for this may be explained by many of the categorical features having
a low number of distinct values. For instance for parity5+5 all the categorical features
are binary with only two values. The number of extra one-hot encoded feature vectors is
therefore not as high as we might otherwise expect. It may also be explained by a rela-
tively low number of unique values for the categorical features and if few values carry a
55
==PAGE:64==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
data signal. Looking at Figure C.1 of the line charts on the regression data sets we also see
that the test R2 scores increases with iterations with approximately the same rate for most
of the 18 data sets with a few exceptions such as 1089 USCrime, 666 rmftsa ladata and
1096 FacultySalaries. The diﬀerence may be explained by the standardisation causing
slight diﬀerences in the best graphs at the early stages of the run of the algorithm, which
lead to convergence to diﬀerent local optima.

Table 7.26 and 7.27 show the ranking score tables for GPLearn. One-hot encoding does
not seem to aﬀect performance much for GPLearn either. Standardisation gives slightly
better scores for the classiﬁcation benchmark, but slightly worse for regression. Looking
at the line chart in Figure C.5 we make an interesting observation, however. On the data
sets 1089 USCrime, 1096 FacultySalaries and 503 wind we see that the test R2 score
starts out much lower for the conﬁguration of GPLearn where the data is standardised.
With training the test score increases, however, and ends up close to the conﬁguration
without standardisation of the data.
Though, on the datasets 659 sleuth ex1714 and
666 rmftsa ladata the test score drops momentarily after around 7 and 4 iterations of
training and for 659 sleuth ex1714 it even ends up at almost -500. These very poor test
scores may be explained by standardisation having an adverse eﬀect on the protected oper-
ators: Because z-score standardisation makes the mean of each feature 0 and variance 1 it
might cause many of the observations to have feature values close to 0, which can cause an
operator such as protected division to output 1 instead of performing the division operation
(see equation (2.1) and the discussion of synthetic test case 2 in Section 7.2). Using an
operator such as the analytic quotient operator [35] in equation (2.4) instead of protected
division might alleviate this slightly, though it generally mimics unprotected division the
most for values further away from 0.

In conclusion, the results for the QLattice and GPLearn seem to conﬁrm our expectation
that the algorithms are able to perform well without standardising the data before training.
This also suggests that symbolic regression in general is robust against diﬀerences in scale of
the features. Going forward with the rest of the benchmarks and for the comparison test in
section 7.4 we will therefore keep the set up simple and not use standardisation or one-hot
encode the categorical variables.

A recent paper by Dick et al. [14] shows that in some cases it can be beneﬁcial to do
z-score standardisation i.e. equation (7.3) in symbolic regression, however. One of their
results is that standardisation can enable a genetic programming algorithm to use a smaller
function set without compromising predictive performance. Another interesting aspect of
their study is that they demonstrate how standardisation can signiﬁcantly improve coeﬃcient
optimisation through gradient descent for genetic programming to produce accurate models.
The coeﬃcient search technique is similar to the QLattice using stochastic gradient descent
to tune weights and bias terms for the input features.
QLattice Semantic Types
We end the investigation of preprocessing by considering the
use of the semantic types in the QLattice feyn interface. When training the QLattice the
user can specify that features are categorical and allow the algorithm to train on data even if
the categorical features are not ordinal encoded [41]. A diﬀerence when using semantic types
is that the QLattice does not allocate weight terms to the categorical feature interactions.
To see to what extent this makes a diﬀerence in performance, we run the QLattice on the
classiﬁcation data sets with and without specifying which features are categorical. Table
7.28 shows the results. Based on these results it seems that the semantic type speciﬁcation
does not help in terms of predictive performance. The model size is lower with semantic
types slightly more often than without semantic types seen by the number of ﬁrst places
and weighted score, but the mean model size is very similar. Closer inspection of the line
chart in Figure C.9 shows that the predictive performance on the three data sets where
the conﬁguration with semantic types achieves the highest test AUC score at the end of
training is not much higher than that of the conﬁguration without semantic types e.g. on
house votes 84 the average test AUC score is approximately 0.99 versus 0.98. On some
data sets such as parity5+5, which only contains binary categorical variables, we see that
the conﬁguration without semantic types ﬁnds a signiﬁcantly better model with a test AUC
score of 0.94 vs 0.4, however, and is able to get an average test AUC score of 1 on monk1
where the conﬁguration with semantic types scores 0.94. Figure 7.29 in this subsection also
shows the predictive performance on the data sets house votes 84, parity5+5, monk1.

7.3.2
Predictive Performance with Iterations
In this subsection we investigate how the predictive performance and model complexity
changes when training the QLattice and GPLearn for many iterations. This will give insight
into to what extent the predictive performance can keep improving and whether or not
bloat will start to develop indicated by an increase in model size without an increase in
predictive performance. The test will also show at which point during training the model
that generalises the best from the train set to the test set is found. For the QLattice and
57

GPLearn we expect the best model to generally be the one when training ﬁnishes regardless
of the number of iterations as more iterations should ideally give more opportunities to ﬁnd
better models. However, for some other algorithms which exhibit a lot of variance unless
heavily regularised such as artiﬁcial neural networks the ability of the model to generalise can
start to degrade after many iterations of training if the model starts to overﬁt the training
data [36]. It is therefore common when ﬁtting neural networks to use a validation set as an
indicator of when the best model has been found during training. The best model is then
taken to be the one with the lowest validation error rather than the model at the end of
training.
QLattice Results
Figure C.11 in the appendix shows a line chart of running the QLattice
for 200 iterations on the regression data sets. The predictive performance of four of the data
sets are also shown in Figure 7.30 We observe that the train and test R2 scores for 15 out of
the 18 regression data sets generally increase with more iterations. The train score is higher,
but for many of the test cases such as 197 cpu act, 503 wind and the Friedman equation
data sets (data sets with fri in the name) the test score is almost on a par with the train
score. This suggests that the QLattice generally does not start to overﬁt after a number
of iterations and the best model in terms of predictive performance can be taken to be the
model after training is ﬁnished.
The exceptions to this trend is seen for the data sets 1089 USCrime and 1096 FacultySalaries
and 659 sleuth ex1714, where the average test score falls after the ﬁrst 25 iterations of
training. Looking at the three data sets in Table 5.1 we see that they only have around 50
observations. This means that for 5-fold cross-validation the train and test sets for each fold
will only contain around 40 and 10 iterations, respectively. It can therefore be diﬃcult to
generalise for these data sets and the presence of outliers can have a high negative impact on
the test scores achieved, which may explain why we see this behaviour for these data sets.
This may also explain the sudden high increase in test score for 1096 FacultySalaries
at the end of training, where the best model in the fold that gave the very low test error
previously got changed enough to not do poorly on the few test observations for that fold.
This also strengthens the claim that training for more iterations is generally favourable in
terms of predictive performance and even after 180 iterations signiﬁcant changes in the best
model can be made.
Figure C.12 in the appendix shows a line chart on the classiﬁcation data sets. Here
we also observe that the train score increases with more iterations and that the test score
generally increases, though slightly more erratically at ﬁrst glance. However, the ﬂuctuations
in test scores are relatively small e.g. on house votes 84 the test score only varies between
0.96 and 0.99.

GPLearn Results
Next we run GPLearn for 200 iterations. Figure C.13 shows a line
chart of the predictive performance. Figure 7.31 shows the predictive performance on the
same subset of four data sets that was included in this subsection for the QLattice. Like
for the QLattice it seems that generally the test R2 score increases or stays the same with
more iterations of training, which suggests that GPLearn is generally also robust against
overﬁtting the training data. For the data sets where GPLearn does the best in terms of test
R2 score such as 197 cpu act, 503 wind, 529 pollen, 595 fri c0 1000 10, the test score
is close to the train score meaning the best model generalises well.
We also observe, however, that GPLearn seems to converge quickly and does not im-
prove much after 50 iterations and the best test R2 scores are generally lower than for the
QLattice for the same data sets. A major diﬀerence in behaviour between GPLearn and
the QLattice seems to be in the beginning of training, where GPLearn often starts out
with very poor solutions, which see a major improvement in train and test R2 scores in
the following iterations. For instance on 197 cpu act the mean test R2 score starts out at
around -8 for GPLearn and above 0.9 for the QLattice, but GPLearn ends up with a very
high test R2 score at the end of training as well. This diﬀerence in behaviour may again be
explained by the weight tuning step of the QLattice, which after the ﬁrst iteration ensures
that weights, scales and bias terms stabilise the random graphs generated initially by the
QLattice component, whereas for GPLearn the graphs are almost completely random after
the ﬁrst iteration.
Looking at the data sets 659 sleuth ex1714 , 1089 USCrime and 1096 FacultySalaries,
which were among the hardest for the QLattice to get a high test R2 score for (except
1089 USCrime), we also see that these prove diﬃcult for GPLearn.
On 1089 USCrime
GPLearn starts out with a much better solution in terms of test score, but most likely
because of a low amount of observations, the best model was swapped out with another
with similar train R2 score in one or more of the cross-validation folds, which did not gener-
alise to the test set. Interestingly the test R2 score is stable for 1096 FacultySalaries, but
as expected we do see a relatively high standard error on the mean, which again indicates a
high variance in the test score between the ﬁve folds.
7.3.3
Model Complexity
We now focus on the model complexity of the QLattice and GPLearn. We consider how the
best model size and the mean model size change throughout training as well as the eﬀect
of using diﬀerent model complexity controlling measures including sorting the QGraph head
using information criteria instead of loss, parsimony pressure and the eﬀect of the hoist
mutation operator for GPLearn.
Model Size Change with Iterations
Figure C.15 and C.16 shows the best and mean
model size change for the QLattice trained for 200 iterations. For many of the data sets

we see that the best model size does not increase after 50 iterations, where it generally

iterations for GPLearn trained for 200 iterations. In terms of general observations we see that
the best model size tends to be slightly higher than the mean model size in the population,
which was the same for the QLattice. The best model size seems to generally ﬂuctuate more
than the QLattice, however, seen by the more jagged blue line (the mean best model size) and
the relatively higher standard error of the mean best model size. Next we observe that for
most data sets where GPLearn did well in terms of test R2 score like 503 wind, 529 pollen,
595 fri c0 1000 10, the best and mean model sizes stabilises at a value between 20 and 40
edges after about 50 iterations. This suggests that GPLearn is able to keep bloat in check
for these data sets even with longer training. For some of the other data sets, however, we
clearly see signs of bloat with the best and mean model sizes increasing after 50 iterations
to very high numbers. For instance on 1089 USCrime and 659 sleuth ex1714, where the
predictive performance was also poor.

for background details on the information criteria). Table 7.35 shows the ranking scores for
regression and Table 7.36 for classiﬁcation. For regression we see that the mean test R2 score

plot of the test R2 scores in Figure 7.34 we also see that all three conﬁgurations achieve high

conﬁgurations except for the small data sets 1089 USCrime and 659 sleuth ex1714, where

the diﬀerence between the test AUC score of loss and BIC is greater.

criteria seem to give smaller models than the conﬁguration sorting on loss. Among AIC and
BIC, BIC ﬁnds the smallest model 16 times for regression and 8 for classiﬁcation. This result
strongly suggests that BIC leads to the simplest models. Because the predictive performance
is still on a par with sorting on loss and AIC, BIC seems to be best choice for the sorting
criterion overall. In [56] Wilstrup and Kasak also found that BIC worked slightly better
than AIC for the QLattice.

Parsimony Pressure
As explained in Section 2.2 parsimony pressure is one of the most
prominent ways in genetic programming of incorporating a model complexity aspect during
training and combatting bloat.
We will therefore make a test for GPLearn for various
degrees of parsimony pressure and see how this aﬀects predictive performance and model
complexity. Further, we will try to apply parsimony pressure to the QLattice by penalising
the ﬁtness of large graphs and compare the results with GPLearn.
We start by running the test for the QLattice, where we train the QLattice for in three
conﬁgurations with a parsimony pressure of 0.01, 0.1 and 1.0. We implement parsimony
pressure for the QLattice by adjusting the loss values of each graph in the QGraph head every
iteration by setting the loss to the loss plus the parsimony coeﬃcient times the parameter
count of the graph. Table 7.37 shows the ranking scores for regression. Form this we clearly
see that too high a parsimony pressure has an adverse eﬀect on the predictive performance,
which may be explained by the models being penalised too much in increasing in complexity
to properly model the data. This is also seen in Figure C.23 and C.23 of the test R2 scores
and best model size on the regression data sets, where a parsimony value of 1.0 results
in a very limited increase in test score with more iterations of training due to a very low
model size; the parsimony values of 0.1 consistently for most data sets results in a lower best
model size compared to 0.01, but at the expense of test R2 score. It might be interesting
in the future to run another test with a lower parsimony pressure setting than 0.01 or use
the number of edges instead of graph parameter count and see if this will lead to better
performance.
For the two data sets 1089 USCrime and 1096 FacultySalaries we see exceptions
though, where the best model size for the parsimony pressure value of 1.0 does increase
with more iterations and the test R2 score is competitive with that of the other conﬁgura-
tions. On 1096 FacultySalaries a parsimony value of 1.0 is the only model that does not
degrade in test R2 with iterations, which may be explained by the simple best model not
changing much after the start of training.

Next we benchmark GPLearn with diﬀerent settings of parsimony pressure. Table 7.39
and Table 7.40 show the the rankings scores. The results are similar to that of the QLattice in
that a lower parsimony pressure gives better predictive performance. However, for GPLearn
we also observe that the parsimony pressure is very critical in keeping the model size in
check. For a parsimony pressure value of 0.0001 which is lower than the default of 0.001, the
best model size also keeps increasing substantially with iterations without much increase in
test R2 score suggesting that a large amount of bloat is developed. The default value of
0.001 in GPLearn seems justiﬁed as it is the second best in terms of predictive performance,
but the best model size does not generally seem to increase after around 20 to 30 iterations
suggesting that the parsimony pressure as intended helps avoid the best model being allowed
to increase in size without enough increase in train loss.

GPLearn Hoist Mutation
We conclude this subsection by examining the eﬀect of the
hoist mutation for GPLearn to see to what extent this operator is important in genetic
programming for controlling model complexity. Recall that the hoist mutation operator can
make trees smaller by replacing subtrees with descendant subtrees. We test four conﬁgu-
rations with the probability of hoist mutation being set to 0.0, 0.01, 0.05 and 0.2. We use
a crossover probability of 0.9 for the ﬁrst three conﬁgurations and 0.75 for the last conﬁg-
uration. The rest of the probability range is designated to a replication operation. Hoist
mutation is therefore the only mutation operator used here. We denote each conﬁguration as
a tuple (pcrossover, phoist mutation) that speciﬁes the crossover and hoist mutation probabilities,
respectively.
Table 7.41 and 7.42 show the ranking scores. From these results there does not appear
to be a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the diﬀerent settings judging by all settings achieving
a decent weighted score. The setting without hoist mutation (0.9, 0.0) achieves a higher
weighted score for predictive performance than the setting with lower crossover probability
and high hoist mutation probability (0.75, 0.2), which would hint that crossover is more
important than hoist mutation for predictive performance, but the diﬀerence is too small
to be deemed signiﬁcant. The mean best model size is very high for the conﬁguration with
probabilities (0.75, 0.2) on the regression test cases, which may be explained by a high
63
==PAGE:72==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
contribution on the one data set 659 sleuth ex1714 when looking at Figure C.32. From
the ﬁgure we also see that the conﬁguration with high hoist mutation probability also leads
to best models of size quite diﬀerent form the other conﬁgurations, where it is quite a bit
lower on some data sets, but also signiﬁcantly higher on others. In conclusion that there
does not seem to be a good reason to adjust the default probabilities in GPLearn for hoist
mutation, which we will keep for the algorithm comparison test in Section 7.4.

7.3.4
Evolutionary Hyper-Parameters
In this subsection we will investigate diﬀerent settings of hyper-parameters for the QLattice
that denote as evolutionary hyper-parameters as they pertain to evolutionary mechanisms
of the algorithm. This includes the mutation strength, graph decay rate and the number
of islands when extracting the best graphs from the QLattice (see section 4.1). We ﬁrst
consider each type parameter separately and then make a test with a combined grid of
the diﬀerent parameter types. The initial benchmarks of each of the evolutionary hyper-
parameters will make it easier to interpret the results as only one parameter is changed
between conﬁgurations and will be faster to run. To enable us to observe how combinations
of the hyper-parameters aﬀect performance we also make a long running benchmark with
the most promising values for the hyper-parameters. The aim of the tests will be to gain
more insight into how good the default values are and if it is possible to ﬁnd better settings
or exclude settings that do not work well.
For GPLearn we also make two benchmarks for a parameter grid of hyper-parameters,
where we ﬁrst analyse parameters pertinent to the suﬃciency of the function set and
ephemeral scalar ranges and then diﬀerent combinations of probabilities of the evolutionary
operators. Lastly we investigate the eﬀect of diﬀerent population sizes. These tests will help
select good parameter settings for the algorithm comparison test in Section 7.4.
QLattice Mutation Strength
We start by investigating the eﬀect of changing the mu-
tation strength of the QLattice. We train the QLattice 25 iterations with three diﬀerent
settings of the mutation strength: 0.1, 1.0, 2.0. The way we let the mutation strength inﬂu-
ence the algorithm in this benchmark is by setting the mutation strength of all interactions
in each of the best graphs that are extracted in ﬁtting loop step 2 (refer to Figure 4.1) at
the end of every iteration.
Table 7.43 and 7.44 show the ranking scores. Based on these test results we cannot
conclude that one setting is much better or worse than the others, but from the regression
rankings the default strength of 1.0 achieves the best scores for test score and model size.
Figures C.35 and C.37 show line charts of the predictive performance and Figures C.36
and C.38 of the best model size. We here get the impression that a strength of 0.1 is less
64

preferred as the best model size is the highest after 25 iterations for some data sets such
as 581 fri c3 500 25 , 633 fri c0 500 25 and quite a bit higher higher than strength 1.0
on 197 cpu act while the predictive performance is not clearly better. However, there is
too much uncertainty to make a deﬁnite conclusion from these plots as well. It would be
interesting to run the test for more than 25 iterations and see the long term eﬀects of the
mutation strength, which might give a better picture of how good the settings are and also
test for more data sets.

QLattice Graph Decay
Next we test a 3 by 3 grid of settings of the parameters A0
and n · τ in the formula for the graph age threshold in equation (4.3). We test the values
[1, 20, 200] for A0, with A0 = 200 being the default. For n·τ we test the values [1, 13.86, 100]
with 13.86 being the default.
Here we utilise that the threshold is only aﬀected by the
product of n and τ so we test values of their product instead of individually, which lowers
the parameter grid size. The parameter values to test have been chosen such that we test
both the default value and values that are lower and higher by around a power of 10.
Figure 7.45 (left) shows a heat map of the test R2 score and test AUC score for each
combination of values of A0 and n · τ for the regression and classiﬁcation data sets. The
test scores are found by ﬁrst computing the mean test score across the 5 cross-validation
folds for every data set and after this the average across all data sets is computed. We
see that the highest test R2 score is achieved for A0 = 200, n · τ = 100 and generally that
the test score is slightly higher with higher A0. The diﬀerence in predictive performance
between the diﬀerent settings is low, however. Looking at the best model size it appears
to be slightly higher for greater values of A0 and lower values of n · τ. A A0 and low n · τ
will give a high threshold for high values of cnt meaning many graphs with a high age that
would otherwise be discarded are allowed to share the same location. This in turn will mean
that the QGraph head will be larger and it appears from this result that that could mean a
graph with slightly larger size is chosen as the best graph.
To get a better idea of how these settings aﬀect the threshold we make a plot of the
graph age threshold for all 9 conﬁgurations (see Figure 7.46). The plot reveals that the
conﬁguration with A0 = 1 give the same threshold curve and as expected we therefore
saw the same test scores and best model size for the conﬁgurations with this setting. In
hindsight some runtime could therefore have been saved by only running one of the three
conﬁgurations where A0 = 1. We do also see diﬀerent types of threshold curves however, so
the results from before are still worth considering.

Lastly we perform benchmarks on
the that aﬀect the number and which graphs are used for updating the QLattice prob-
ability distribution every iteration.
