QLattice Large Grid
Having performed an initial set of benchmarks of evolutionary
hyper-parameters of the QLattice, we now make a test with a grid that includes multiple
hyper-parameters. This test will therefore attempt to discover any potential interrelation-
ship between the parameters that the previous tests did not pick up on. We will also choose
slightly diﬀerent values for some of the parameters, which seem seem more promising. Be-
cause the previous tests gave similar results we will run this test with a diﬀerent setup that
includes more data sets. We run the test on all regression PMLB data sets that have at
most 200 features and 10000 observations. Further, to prioritise non synthetic data sets we
only include data sets not based on the friedman equation, that is, all regression data sets
that do not contain fri in their name. In total this amounts to 55 data sets. Note that
this subset of PMLB data sets include both data sets from Table 5.1, Table 5.2 and more.
Despite saying earlier that we would only in Section 7.3 train on the data sets in Table 5.1
to avoid biasing the optimal parameter choice on the test data sets used in the performance
comparison in Section 7.4, this test will not violate this as this test was carried out towards
the end of the project after the performance comparison of the algorithms. We benchmark
the following parameter grid:
1 ’strength ’: [0.5 , 1.0, 1.5] ,
2 ’A0’: [100 , 200, 300] ,
3 ’n * tau’: [13.86 , 60, 100] ,
4 ’island
maximum
distance ’: [1, 3, 10]
Initial testing with a large grid of the evolutionary hyper-parameters showed that the results
were still quite inconclusive when only the data sets in Table 5.1 were used, hence why this
test was set up diﬀerently. With the addition of more data sets and number of parameter
conﬁgurations the runtime of the benchmark increases signiﬁcantly, so we make the choice
of lowering the number of cross-validation folds in this particular test to 2. This means
that the average test score will have higher variance for a given data set, but will also mean
that it will be easier to generalise from the train set to the test set on small data sets such
as 1096 FacultySalaries and 659 sleuth ex1714. Even with only 2 folds per data set it
took 7 full days of training to complete the benchmark.
67
==PAGE:76==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
To visualise the result we will use parallel coordinate plots using the functionality of
Plotly15. This will allow us to see the aggregated performance of all conﬁgurations in the
same plot. Each line in a parallel coordinate plot corresponds to a run of the algorithm with
the conﬁguration of hyper-parameters indicated by where the line intersects with the vertical
bars. Each vertical bar represents a parameter indicated by the label at the top. The right
most bar shows the performance metric score of each conﬁguration. As before we examine
both the the predictive performance by the average test R2 score and model complexity
by the average best model size. The colour of each line shows the relative magnitude of
the performance metric based on the colour bar shown to the right. To better be able to
distinguish the lines from one another a small random oﬀset is added to the point where
each line intersects with the vertical lines.
There are two interesting ways of aggregating the results across all the data sets:
1. Computing the average performance metric on each data set, setting it to 0 if it is
negative, and then aggregate across all data sets. This is the same approach as we use
when we compute the mean test scores and mean best model size in the ranking score
tables.
2. Computing the average performance metric on each data set and then aggregate across
all data sets. If the total average is negative we set it to 0 when visualising the result.
To gain the most detailed view of the results we will visualise the results using both of these
two ways of aggregating. The ﬁrst approach will be robust against outliers in performance
on some data sets, whereas the second approach will pick up on this. We refer to these two
ways of aggregating as aggregation (1) and (2).
Figure C.47 shows the results for the predictive performance (test R2 score) using ag-
gregation method (1) and Figure C.50 using aggregation (2). Starting with Figure C.50 we
see that most conﬁguration achieve an average test R2 score between 0.60 and 0.68, but also
that there are some conﬁguration that got a very poor test R2 score. On Figure C.47 we
see that the low test score is less pronounced with the worst conﬁguration achieving a test
R2 score of around 0.63. Closer inspection of the computation of the results reveal that one
conﬁguration got a test R2 score of -171.87 on 228 elusage, which explains why the total av-
erage was set to 0 in the parallel coordinate plot. Interestingly only three conﬁgurations got
a negative score on 228 elusage, but we also see negative test R2 scores on other data sets
such as 542 pollution, 485 analcatdata vehicle and 678 visualizing environmental
for multiple conﬁgurations, though less severe.
To get a better view of what performs the best and the worst we plot the lines for
the best and worst conﬁgurations in separate plots.
Figure C.48 (aggregation (1)) and
C.51 (aggregation (2)) show the 10 conﬁgurations that achieved the highest test R2 score.
From these plots we see that conﬁgurations with all values of the hyper-parameters tested
are present, but that there are more conﬁgurations with a island maximum distance of
10, strength of 1.0 or 1.5 and n · τ of 60 judging by the number of lines intersecting at
these values.
We also see that the two conﬁgurations with the highest test R2 score of
approximately 0.68 are: A0 = 100, n · τ = 60, island maximum distance = 10, strength
= 1.5 and A0 = 200, n · τ = 60, island maximum distance = 1, strength = 1.0 though
there are other conﬁgurations very close in predictive performance as well. Figure C.49
(aggregation (1)) and C.52 (aggregation (2)) show the 5 conﬁgurations that achieved the
lowest test R2 score. We only show the 5 worst as there 5 conﬁgurations for aggregation (2)
that did considerably worse than the other conﬁgurations. We observe that none of the 5
worst conﬁgurations had a mutation strength of 1.5, which suggests that a lower mutation
15https://plotly.com/python-api-reference/generated/plotly.express.parallel_coordinates.
html (accessed April 2021).
A drawback of using Plotly is that it requires a paid license to export the
ﬁgures as PDF. We will therefore make due with a PNG ﬁle format for these plots, but allocate an entire
page in the appendix to alleviate the issue as best as possible.
68
==PAGE:77==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
strength than the default of 1.0 is undesirable and that it might be beneﬁcial to increase it
slightly instead.
Finally we consider the eﬀect on the model complexity shown in Figure C.53. From
this plot we see that conﬁgurations with a mutation strength of 1.5 and island maximum
distance of 10 seem to lead to smaller best models. We also see that the default value of
n · τ = 13.86 seems to lead to more complex models compared to the conﬁgurations that
with higher values of n · τ = 60 or n · τ = 100. The worst performing conﬁguration in terms
of model complexity is A0 = 100, n · τ = 13.86, island maximum distance = 1, strength =
1.5, which may be explained by too much exploitation and too little exploration with island
maximum distance = 1, strength = 1.5 and low A0.
Based on these results it seems that increasing the product n·τ above the default value of
13.86 and increasing the mutation strength will give simpler models without compromising
predictive performance.
For longer training sessions than 25 iterations it might also be
worth increasing A0 as an increase in n · τ will result in the graph threshold dropping to 0
faster after which it will not change and increasing A0 will make the graph threshold have
an eﬀect for longer.
GPLearn Suﬃciency and Evolutionary Operators
We end this Section with bench-
marking a grid of hyper-parameters for GPLearn as well, which will give more insight into
the best parameter setting as well as how much diﬀerence in performance the diﬀerent
parameters lead to.
We perform a benchmark of the following two parameter grids for GPLearn:
1 # Grid 1
2 ’const_range ’: [( -10.0 ,
10.0) , (-1.0, 1.0) , (-0.1, 0.1)],
3 ’init_depth ’: [(1, 2), (2, 6), (6, 7)],
4 ’function_set ’: [(’add’, ’mul ’), (’add ’, ’sub ’), (’add ’, ’sub ’, ’mul ’, ’div ’), utils.
gplearn_all_functions_available ]
5
6 # Grid 2
7 ’tournament_size ’: [2, 20, 200] ,
8 ’p_crossover ’: [0.25 , 0.9] ,
9 ’p_subtree_mutation ’: [0.01 , 0.25] ,
10 ’p_point_mutation ’: [0.01 , 0.25] ,
11 ’p_point_replace ’: [0.05 , 0.25] ,
The ﬁrst grid includes parameters aﬀecting the suﬃciency and closure of the algorithm with
diﬀerently sized function sets and ephemeral constant ranges. The initial depth is included
as well to see if the diﬀerent function set sizes beneﬁt from smaller or larger initial trees. The
speciﬁc values in the ﬁrst grid have been chosen so they include the default setting as well
as a smaller and higher value. The second grid includes diﬀerent settings of the probabilities
of the evolutionary operators with a low and a high value chosen for each parameter. Those
conﬁgurations with a sum of probabilities greater than 1 are not run as it is undeﬁned. The
grids are shown above with the parameter keywords as they appear in GPLearn. Refer to
the GPLearn documentation for all details of the meaning of the parameters16 [50].
We only consider predictive performance for these two parameter grids as we will focus
on choosing the parameter setting of GPLearn that gives the most competitive predictive
performance when comparing the QLattice with GPLearn in Section 7.4. Figure C.54 and
C.55 in the appendix show parallel coordinate plots for the results for the ﬁrst grid. We only
render the parallel coordinate plots here using aggregation (1) (refer to the previous para-
graph on the use of parallel coordinate plots for the QLattice evolutionary hyper-parameter
grid). Since the parallel coordinate plot functionality used to render the plot only allows
the use of numbers on the vertical lines, the integers 0, 1, 2 and 3 have been chosen to
represent parameter setting given by the corresponding order in the grid listed above. The
order is also shown in the caption of the ﬁgures. The ﬁrst ﬁgure for the test R2 score on
the regression test cases and the second ﬁgure for the test AUC score on the classiﬁcation
test cases. From these results we clearly see that the full function set performs the best,
16https://gplearn.readthedocs.io/en/stable/reference.html (accessed March 2021)
69
==PAGE:78==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
which makes good sense as the elementary operators on their own can be diﬃcult to model
many kinds of more advanced structure in data accurately with a small or medium sized
model. An interesting observation here is also that the function set with addition and mul-
tiplication performs relatively well on the regression test cases; signiﬁcantly better than the
conﬁgurations with a function set of addition and subtraction only. This may be explained
by GPLearn building very large trees and can compensate for not having functions such
as division by doing multiplication with scalars close to 0 and compensate for not having
subtraction by using negative scalars.
Figure C.56 and C.57 show parallel coordinate plots for the results of the second grid
with the ﬁrst ﬁgure for the regression cases and the second on the classiﬁcation cases. Here
see clearly that a tournament size of 20 gives better results than 200 which makes sense as
too high a tournament size makes the search too exploitive and can lead to quick convergence
to a suboptimal solution which lie close the the best individual from the ﬁrst few iterations.
GPLearn Population Size
Unlike the QLattice, GPLearn has a ﬁxed population size,
which is 1000 by default. We now test the performance of GPLearn with a small population
sizes of 10 and 100 as well as a large population size of 10000.
We expect the smaller
population sizes to do considerably worse because of too large increase in the bias of the
algorithm. A large population size is expected to be at least as good as the default population
size, but will also increase the running time in CPU time of the algorithm as there are more
individuals in the population to evaluate the ﬁtness of.
Table 7.51 and Table 7.52 show the rankings. The results show that a larger population
size gives better test scores, but the best model size is also much larger. There therefore
seems to be a trade-oﬀbetween predictive performance and interpretability for the pop-
ulation size parameter.
An interesting observation here is also that GPLearn is able to
generalise well with large models as the test error is high despite the large high best model
size. This may be explained by some redundant substructures being included in the best
model in addition to useful substructures. The redundant substructures cause a larger model
size but do not lead to overﬁtting as they do not aﬀect the output by too much. For in-
stance on 197 cpu act and 503 wind in Figure C.58 and C.59 the model size increases after
10 iterations but the test R2 score remains the same. We do observe, however, that after
around 20 iterations the best model size generally does not increase much, which suggests
that bloat is not added to the best model uncontrollably. The large model size can also be
because larger subtrees are developed which represent better sub-expressions. For instance
the weights for certain input features can be made more accurate by combining multiple
ephemeral scalars.
Although not apparent from the results documented here the runtime in seconds for the
conﬁguration with a population size of 10000 took noticeably longer than the default value
of 1000, which makes good sense as there are 10 times as many trees in the population to
evaluate the ﬁtness of. There is therefore also a trade-oﬀbetween predictive performance
per iteration and wall-clock time spent per iteration.

7.3.5
Diversity
In this subsection we will investigate the diversity of the QLattice in comparison with
GPLearn by inspecting how a number of the diversity metrics from Table 2.1 change through-
out training. Furthermore, we will apply diversity promoting methods from genetic pro-
gramming literature to the QLattice and see how this aﬀects the performance. We limit the
benchmarks to only run on the regression test cases as some of the benchmarks are very
time consuming to run.
Variety
We start by comparing the variety in the QGraph head and population of genetic
programming at every iteration. Recall that the variety of a population of graphs is the
percentage of unique graphs in the population (see Section 2.2.4).
For graphs from the
QLattice algorithm we will consider two graphs the same if and only the type of interactions
and edges in the graphs are the same. We therefore disregard the weight, scale and bias
terms for the input feature and linear interactions in the graphs. We do this since we want
to capture the structure of the graphs and because small diﬀerences in the weight terms
would otherwise mean that two graphs with the exact same interactions and edges would be
perceived as diﬀerent. For genetic programming it makes more sense to include ephemeral
scalars as these are dedicated nodes tree introduced through the evolutionary operators and
do not change unless they are targeted again by an evolutionary operation. The choice was
made to also not take the location of the interactions of the graphs from the QLattice into
account when computing the variety even though they play a role in terms of similarity
of two graphs. The reason for this is that the QLattice every iteration as part of ﬁtting
loop step 1 in algo x discards graphs with the same hash code. If the locations would be
included the variety would likely be the same as the hash code of the graphs, meaning the
variety metric would just describe the amount of new graphs generated by the QLattice in
ﬁtting loop step 4.
Figure C.62 shows the variety computed for the QLattice and GPLearn run for 200
iterations. We immediately observe a diﬀerence between the two algorithms with the variety
increasing for the QLattice with more iterations of training until it converges to a value
between 0.8 and 0.9 while the variety for most data sets for GPLearn decreases with more
iterations of training until converging after about 50 iterations to a value that seems to be
between 0.2 and 0.4 for most data sets. This means that after 50 iterations only between 200
and 400 of the 1000 individuals in the population are unique. As described in Section 2.2.4
too many similar trees in the population for a genetic programming algorithm means the
search will be more local. It therefore also makes sense that we saw in Figure C.13 that the
test R2 score did not change much after 50 iterations. Conversely, for the QLattice we see
a high variety even after 50 iterations, which may be explained by the QLattice algorithm
discarding identical graphs every iteration. It makes good sense that the variety is not 100
% as the QLattice may generate graphs with a structure that already exists in the QGraph
head in ﬁtting loop step 4.
Overlap Distance
The variety gives a very strict measure of the diversity in the popula-
tion of graphs. We therefore also consider how the overlap distance (see Table 2.1) changes
throughout training, which is able to pick up on graphs that are not identical but still have
a very similar structure. Initial testing with the overlap distance gave some huge values for
71
==PAGE:80==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
GPLearn for some data sets such as 659 sleuth ex1714 because the population included
many small and many very large trees. Moreover, because the diversity metric itself is the
mean overlap distance squared. This made it diﬃcult to compare the development between
iterations and plotting the logarithm of mean overlap distance only partially alleviated the
issue. The choice was therefore made to adapt the diversity metric by normalising with
the larger of the two trees that are compared instead of normalising by the smaller tree.
This means the metric is no longer true to the original formulation of the overlap distance
metric as in [12], but it still reﬂects the similarity between two graphs and the distances
are brought to a much lower scale making it easier to plot. A drawback of this convenience
change is that larger overlap diﬀerences between two trees will be less pronounced in the
plot of diversity that we make.
Figure C.63 shows the adapted mean squared overlap distance for the QLattice and
GPLearn. Only 16 data sets are shown as the time spent computing the metric for the
data sets 659 sleuth ex1714 and 1089 USCrime was too high for GPLearn for the test to
ﬁnish within a reasonable amount of time. Even without these data sets the benchmark
took a total of 44.7 hours for the QLattice and 89.5 hours for GPLearn to complete, which
is quite a bit longer than the running the test without computing the overlap distance. This
highlights the major drawback of the distance, which is also reﬂected in the asymptotic
upper bound on the runtime in Table 2.1, namely that the upper bound on the running time
is polynomial in the size of the largest tree, Tmax, in the population and polynomial in the
size of the population. For the data sets 659 sleuth ex1714 in particular this is a problem
as the mean model size reaches more than 2000 nodes after just 50 iterations (seen by Figure
C.17). Even though the runtime bound is an upper bound, it seems tight in practice.
Looking at the adapted mean squared overlap distance we see the same tendency for the
QLattice and GPLearn as we saw with the variety: The diversity increases for the QLattice
and decreases for GPLearn during training. For GPLearn the metric seems to converge to
around 0.5 after around 50 iterations.
Fitness Variance
Finally we consider a the behavioural diversity metric ﬁtness variance,
which like the variety is simple and fast to compute, but is also a very coarse grained way
of measuring diversity. We compute the ﬁtness variance by going through all graphs in
the QGraph head of the QLattice and population for GPLearn and computing the variance
of their loss values on the train set. For the QLattice the loss value denotes the squared
error and for GPLearn the loss value denotes the absolute error, which are the default loss
values for the two algorithms. The loss is therefore expected to be higher for the QLattice
than for GPLearn. Figure C.64) and C.65 show the ﬁtness variance for the QLattice and
GPLearn, respectively. For the QLattice the results are only shown for some data sets as
some graphs has a loss value of NaN. For the remaining data sets we see that there is very
high ﬁtness variance. The ﬁtness variance changes by a lot throughout training with some
iterations having a very high ﬁtness variance and some having a much lower ﬁtness variance.
For GPLearn the ﬁtness variance spikes for some iteration but does not change as much for
most iterations. The ﬁtness variance is generally lower as expected because the absolute
error is used instead of squared error for each graph and the spikes in ﬁtness variance may
be explained by some trees being generated by addition of some very poor functions that
do not work well with the problem. The ﬁtness value then decreases again next iteration as
the poor trees are not included in the next generation. Interesting to observe is also that for
GPLearn the ﬁtness variance is generally higher after the very ﬁrst iteration, which makes
sense as there more poor performing trees in the initial population. For the QLattice it is
not the case as much that the ﬁrst iteration has the highest ﬁtness variance as the weights
for the features are tuned in the ﬁrst iteration and the graphs will therefore have had a
chance to adapt the training set.
72
==PAGE:81==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
QLattice Best Graphs
As mentioned Section 4.1 the best graphs in ﬁtting loop step 3
of the QLattice are extracted in sorted order but subject to a constraint to how close the
output interaction location can be to other top graphs extracted. The idea is then that
this will promote more diverse graphs being extracted and used for updating the QLattice
graph probability distribution. To investigate to what extent the performance is aﬀected
by this, we run the QLattice in its default conﬁguration and in a conﬁguration where we
extract the ﬁrst 10 graphs of the sorted QGraph head instead; that is, we do not invoke the
method feyn. qgraph.best. For ease of reference we refer to these two conﬁgurations as
head and .best() The ranking scores are seen in Table 7.53 and 7.54 for regression and
classiﬁcation. From this we see that conﬁguration .best() generally leads to slightly smaller
models judging by the number of ﬁrst places. Looking at Figure C.66 of the test R2 score
we see that the predictive performance is very similar in how it develops during training.
Looking at Figure C.68 of the best model size we observe that for some data sets the best
model size keeps increasing after 15 iterations for the head conﬁguration, but remains the
same with the constraint while they both see around the same increase in test R2 score (data
sets 633 fri c0 500 25 and 618 fri c3 1000 50). As the test score is similar between the
two conﬁgurations it therefore suggests that the conﬁguration with .best() is using a better
graph structure that can generalise just as well, but using fewer edges.
Overall the results favour using .best(), where the better model size may be explained
by more exploration of the search space. To see a greater performance diﬀerence, however,
we would need to run on more data sets and for more iterations as diversity promotion would
then get a better chance of taking eﬀect.

QLattice using Genetic Marker Diversity Promotion
In [10] the authors showed
how the the genetic marker approach described in 2.2.4 could help avoid premature con-
vergence for genetic programming for a number of problems, one of which was a symbolic
regression problem. We will now apply this technique for the QLattice to see if this tech-
nique will also be beneﬁcial for performance for QLattice on the chosen PMLB data sets.
We run the QLattice in four conﬁgurations:
• Default QLattice conﬁguration
• Using a depth of 1 when computing the genetic marker density
• Using a depth of 1 when computing the genetic marker density and extracting the
best graphs in ﬁtting loop step 2 as the ﬁrst 10 in the QGraph head instead of calling
feyn. qgraph.best()
• Using a depth of 3 when computing the genetic marker density
73
==PAGE:82==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
Details of the implementation of the genetic marker approach for the QLattice are presented
in 6. The default QLattice conﬁguration will give the baseline performance. A depth of
3 will yield smaller densities as more graphs will have diﬀerent genetic markers, which
we will compare with using a depth of 1.
For depth 1 we also include a conﬁguration
using the QGraph head, which will more closely mimic how the algorithm would work for a
genetic programming based algorithm even though the results from the previous paragraph
suggested that using feyn. qgraph.best() works the best.
Table 7.55 shows the ranking scores on the regression test cases. We see a similar mean
test score, but the default conﬁguration of the QLattice achieves a better weighted score and
number of ﬁrst places. Looking at the model size we observe that the standard conﬁguration
does the worst in terms of model size, however, and that using the genetic marker approach
with a depth of 1 or 3 seems to give simpler best models. The line chart in Figure C.71 shows
that the test R2 score is higher by between 0.1 and 0.02 for 13 of the data sets, but is lower
on 1096 FacultySalaries, which causes the mean test R2 score to be similar. Figure C.72
of the best model size shows clearly that for most data sets the model size is considerably
lower for the conﬁguration using genetic markers of depth 1 or 3. For multiple data sets the
number of edges is half that of the default QLattice conﬁguration and for data sets such as
197 cpu act the best model size is only 10, but the test R2 score is only worse by around
0.15 more than the default QLattice conﬁguration which has 35 edges.
It is interesting how well the QLattice with the genetic marker approach performs in
predictive performance considering that it changes the way the QLattice runs by default by
quite a bit. It is also interesting how it leads to simpler models. It is important to note,
however, that the QLattice in this test is sorting on loss and optimises for predictive perfor-
mance primarily. Had we used the BIC sorting criterion instead or chosen a smaller model
in the QGraph head it would also lead to simpler model without compromising predictive
performance by too much. Nevertheless, it could be interesting to make further experiments
with ideas from genetic marker optimisation for the QLattice and run for more iterations.
The genetic marker density is also a useful metric in itself for measuring diversity in the
QGraph head even when no optimisation for promoting diversity is done.

QLattice Using Novelty Search
Despite novelty search having been used for genetic
programming with success [29], we do not expect to see it perform better than the default
search method for the QLattice. Lehman and Stanley [30, 29] argue that novelty search is
generally the most applicable in domains with deceptive suboptimal solutions. Although
there are many local minima in the ﬁtness landscape in symbolic regression, the landscape
is not deceptive in the same sense as for instance a maze like environment, which is one
of the domains the authors test on when applying the method for genetic programming.
Moreover, for the QLattice and symbolic regression there is not a strong notion of diﬀerent
behaviour. Two behavioural diversity metrics were considered in Table 2.1, but the ﬁtness
variance implemented as part of this project did not seem so usable. Nevertheless, we run the
QLattice with an adapted implementation of the novelty search algorithm (see Section 2.2.4)
out of interest and if it is worth working more with in the future. Since ﬁtness variance did
not seem promising for measuring diversity we will instead use the overlap distance metric
even though it is structural instead of behavioural. We set the probability of adding a graph
to the archive of graphs to 0.05 (refer to Section 2.2.4), the value k in equation (2.7) is to
74
==PAGE:83==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
10. To see if novelty search improves search after many iterations we train the QLattice for
50 iterations instead of 25.
Initial testing showed that the runtime of the QLattice with novelty search got very
high with more iterations caused by the overlap distance metric, the archive increasing in
size and the that we train for 50 instead of 25 iterations. As a compromise we therefore
relax the novelty part of the search by only sorting the ﬁrst 50 graphs of QGraph head in
descending order with respect to the overlap distance metric. We do this resorting right
after ﬁtting loop step 1 where the QGraph is sorted on loss as usual. Further, we only run
the algorithm on four selected data sets: 1030 ERA, 1096 FacultySalaries, 197 cpu act,
659 sleuth ex1714.
The data sets were chosen based on previous experiments showing
diﬀerent kind of development of the test R2 score with iterations.
Figure 7.56 and 7.57 show the predictive performance and model size. The predictive
performance seems similar or slightly inferior to the default algorithm (see Section 7.3.2).
To properly test the algorithm it seems that only resorting the ﬁrst 50 graphs in the QGraph
head is not enough and that the overlap distance might not be the best diversity metric
to use. Instead of pursuing this further, however, time and eﬀort is spent elsewhere on the
project.

7.3.6
Initialisation
For both the QLattice and GPLearn an initial set of random solutions are generated, which
form the basis for the rest of the search. With a good set of initial solutions the search has
better chances of exploring the most promising areas of the search space more and converge
to an optimal solution. On the other hand bad initial solutions can increase the risk of the
search converging to a suboptimal solution.
It is therefore interesting to compare how good the initial solutions generated by the
QLattice and GPLearn. To this end, we initialise the QLattice and GPLearn with default
75
==PAGE:84==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
parameters and record the predictive performance, model size and diversity of the initial
graphs generated before any training has taken place. We evaluate the algorithms on all
observations in each data set as the initial graphs have not been ﬁtted to any data.
Predictive Performance
We extract all 1000 graphs from the initial population in
GPLearn and the ﬁrst 1000 graphs from the unsorted QGraph head for the QLattice. Because
no ﬁtting has taken place the R2 scores will be negative for many graphs. We therefore use
RMSE as a predictive performance measure instead, which will only give positive values.
Note that unlike the R2 score, lower is better. Graphs that give NaN (invalid numeric values)
values are assigned an RMSE of 1032.
Figure 7.58 shows the RMSE for the QLattice and GPLearn on the 18 data sets. We
can see that the error is lower for most graphs for the QLattice, but some of the graphs
produces NaN values contrary to GPLearn, though some of the graphs from GPLearn give
a very high loss value still for some data sets. In terms of loss it seems the variance and
spread in values is greater for GPLearn.

Diversity
We also consider the diversity in the initial population. Table 7.2 shows for each
data set the variety, mean overlap distance for the ﬁrst 1000 graphs of the unsorted QGraph
head of the QLattice and for the initial population of 1000 trees for GPLearn (abbreviated

and GPLearn are shown in the bottom most two rows.

much bigger and scalars included in the trees explicitly as mentioned before. The overlap
distance is around the same, but slightly higher for GP for all data sets except 503 wind.
This suggests that the diversity of the ﬁrst 1000 graphs in the QGraph and 1000 graphs in
the population of GPLearn is actually around the same.

Genetic Programming Initialisation Methods
Lastly, we make an experiment for the
three initialisation methods common in genetic programming to see how much of a diﬀerence
in performance it gives during training for GPLearn. We benchmark three conﬁgurations of
GPLearn with the initialisation scheme being ramped half-and-half (see Section 2.2), grow
and full.
Grow here means only initialising the population using the grow method and
full meaning only generating complete trees. We use a default initial depth range of [2, 6]
which will give diﬀerently sized trees. Table 7.60 shows the rankings. In terms of predictive
performance there does not appear to be a signiﬁcant diﬀerence with all three initialisation
methods achieving a high weighted score.
Surprisingly, the ramped half-and-half gets a
slightly lower mean test R2 score and higher mean best model size. Looking more closely
at Figure C.74 of the predictive performance we see that the test R2 score for the ramped
77
==PAGE:86==
7.3
Algorithm Settings Tests
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
half-and-half initialisation method is signiﬁcantly better on some data data sets such as
529 pollen and 618 fri c3 1000 50, but much worse on others e.g. 643 fri c2 500 25
and 666 rmftsa ladata. We also observe that after only 10 to 20 iterations the best and
worst conﬁgurations are the same as they are after 50 iterations. These observations strongly
suggest that the initial population in GPLearn has a high inﬂuence on the performance of
the algorithm at the end of training. It might therefore be advantageous to run the algorithm
in parallel in a number of instances with diﬀerent random seeds and initialisation methods
for 20 iterations and from then on continue training for only the model that achieves the
highest training error.

7.3.7
Restricted Search Space
We conclude Section 7.3 by running two experiments of restricting the search space for the
QLattice and investigating how the performance is aﬀected. This will give insight into how
well the data can be modelled by simpler models and if a more limited search space can be
beneﬁcial. We start by running the QLattice with the following three function sets:
• Add, multiply
• Add, multiply, linear, inverse
• All functions in function set included (default)
Table 7.62 and 7.63 shows the ranking scores. From this we see that like for GPLearn the
QLattice performs the best with the full function set with a considerably higher weighted
score on the regression test cases for both predictive performance and best model size. For
the classiﬁcation test cases the function set with addition, multiplication, linear and inverse
function types the performance is on par with the full function set, however, which may be
explained by those data sets being enough to model the data.

Next we run a test where we impose a constraint on the number of edges of graphs in
the QGraph head. We use the values 4, 10, 16, 22 where 4 is considered quite low and 22 is
unlikely to make much of a diﬀerence since the best graphs graphs will likely have fewer edges
anyway. Also, we remark that the QLattice by default has a maximum depth restriction of
5 meaning the maximum number of edges possible would be 25 = 32. Table 7.62 and 7.63
shows the ranking scores. The scores show that restricting the maximum number of edges
leads to a decrease in predictive performance, but as expected lowers the model size. We
see that there is not much diﬀerence between a maximum of 16 edges and 22 edges meaning
that the QLattice will often choose best models with at most 16 edges, which is already a
low model size.

In conclusion based on the results from these tests and the experiment involving function
sets for GPLearn, it seems that the PMLB regression data requires more advanced functions
than just the four elementary operators addition, subtraction, multiplication and division
and that graphs for the QLattice with more than 10 edges but less than 16 edges do the
best in terms of test scores.
7.4
Algorithm Performance Comparison
We will now carry out the performance test where we compare the QLattice, GPLearn and
Eureqa. We compare predictive performance, tendency to overﬁt and the best models found
on some of the data sets. Model complexity is not compared in this test as there did not
seem to be an easy way in the Datarobot Python API to extract the best model size and it
would be time-consuming to implement a parser for the Eureqa model expressions in order
to compute it ourselves. The comparison of the best models will still provide some insight
into how the model complexities compare, however.
Test Setup
We use the two-layer evaluation loop presented in Section 5.2 seen in Figure
5.5 (right). We repeat the outer-layer 3 times and use 2 folds for the inner cross-validation
layer. When determining the best conﬁguration for an algorithm in the inner layer we train
79
==PAGE:88==
7.4
Algorithm Performance Comparison
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
the algorithm in each conﬁguration for 25 iterations. We benchmark on the data sets in
Table 5.2.
Table 7.3 shows the conﬁgurations for each algorithm we benchmark. For the QLattice
we use the default conﬁguration which the previous results showed worked well and the best
conﬁguration used in [56]. For GPLearn we include the algorithm with default parameters
and a conﬁguration with all functions available included in the function set and a population
size of 1500. Including more functions in the function set was found to be considerably better
for GPLearn; a higher population size also worked better in terms of predictive performance,
but we only keep it at 1500 to not increase the running time of the algorithm signiﬁcantly.
For Eureqa we only use the default parameter setting as no previous parameter testing has
been made for this algorithm that warrants a change of the default parameters.
Algorithm
Parameter Settings Diﬀerent From Default
QLattice
-
QLattice
Sorting on BIC information criterion, maximum number of edges set to 11
GPLearn
-
GPLearn
All functions in function set used, population size set to 1500
Eureqa
-
Table 7.3: Settings for algorithm conﬁgurations that are included in performance comparison test.
The
default parameter for the respective algorithms are used unless otherwise stated.
Note that the default
parameter setting is here what the algorithms use in the Python modules feyn version 1.5.3, gplearn
version 0.4.1 and datarobot version 2.24.0.
Number of Iterations of Training
From the experiment in Section 7.3.2 we concluded
for both the QLattice and GPLearn that the algorithms seemed to get better with more
iterations and there was no need for using part of the training data as a validation set to
determine early stopping. We will therefore train each model as many iterations as pos-
sible and take the best model to be that at the end of training. In order to determine a
fair number of maximum iterations for each algorithm we make a quick preliminary inspec-
tion of the runtime of each algorithm conﬁguration. Because of the way the API works
for Eureqa it is advantageous to choose the default number of iterations, which is 3000,
and ﬁnd a number of iterations that gives the same amount of runtime for the other al-
gorithms. However, the runtime of Eureqa for the same conﬁguration varies depending on
the time it takes for the training job to start in the queue and the number of CPU cores
and memory allocated. For instance for 537 houses (regression, 20640 observations) and
AMETES Epistasis 2 Way 20atts 0.4H EDM 1 1 (classiﬁcation, 1600 observations) it took
approximately 3 minutes, but for kr vs kp (classiﬁcation, 3196 observations) it took 30
minutes and for Breast cancer winsconsin (classiﬁcation 3000 generations, 569 observa-
tions) it took more than an hour. Generally the impression was that 3 minutes was the case
most of the time for most data sets. Running the QLattice in its default conﬁguration for
50 iterations took 4 minutes, which was deemed a fair amount of training time. GPLearn
with a population size of 1500 trained for 60 iterations takes around 4 minutes as well, so
this number of iterations was chosen for GPLearn.
Test Scores on Each Data Set
Figure 7.66 shows the test R2 score for the 19 regression
data sets for each algorithm and Figure 7.67 shows the test AUC score for the 9 classiﬁcation
data sets. We see that the QLattice and Eureqa both generally receive high test scores and
are never much worse than the second best. GPLearn scores well for some data sets such as
344 mv but is considerably worse on others. For the classiﬁcation data sets all the algorithms
do well in terms of test AUC scores. It makes sense that we do not see very bad scores for
GPLearn for classiﬁcation as the output values are between 0 and 1 and it is therefore not
80
==PAGE:89==
7.4
Algorithm Performance Comparison
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
possible to get a very high error due to a few very bad estimates. Surprisingly GPLearn
even scores the highest among the three algorithms for data sets glass2, horse colic and
spambase, though the diﬀerence in test AUC scores is very low and may be explained by
those data sets not being diﬃcult to ﬁt a good model on with the right choice of features.

Train versus Test Scores
We now investigate the ability of the algorithms to generalise
in a little more detail. The test scores already provide a measure of the ability of the models
to generalise, but we will now see to what extent the models overﬁt the training training

Best Models Inspection
We compare the best models found for a subset of the data
set to get a rough idea of how similar the best models are and how they compare in what
functions are used.
Figures 7.72, 7.73, 7.74 show the best models found in the ﬁrst of
the three Monte-Carlo runs for the QLattice, GPLearn and Eureqa, respectively, for four
selected data sets.
First consider the data set 574 house 16H which is the regression data set the QLattice
and Eureqa got the lowest test R2 score. The best model found by the QLattice interestingly
only uses two input gaussian functions, which combine ﬁve input features. We can see that
Eureqa similar to the QLattice at the leaf nodes scales the input features P27p4, H13p1 with
a multiplication with a high weight as well as using the logarithm of P1, product of P27p4
and H8p2 and H13p1 features. These are then combined with the elementary operators
addition, subtraction, division and multiplication. Comparing the formulas, Eureqa seems
to have a slightly simpler model on this data set, but the models are comparable in predictive
performance. We see that the best model of GPLearn is very complex in comparison with
the QLattice and Eureqa and that the test R2 score is negative.
Next we consider 344 mv which is the data set where both the QLattice, GPLearn and
Eureqa achieved the highest test R2 score. Comparing the three models we see that GPLearn
ﬁnds arguably the simplest model as it only uses addition, subtraction and multiplication
to combine three input features with four internal nodes. The QLattice also ﬁnds a simple
model which uses four input features. Eureqa here uses an if-then-else function, which
takes three inputs.
Now consider the data set arg1690 visualising galaxy, where the QLattice and Eu-
reqa achieve very high test R2 scores, but GPLearn achieves its lowest test R2 score among
all the data sets. We see that the best model of GPLearn contains a chain of alternating
applications of the tanh and sine functions. Similar behaviour could have been achieved
with a much simpler structure, so this shows that some redundancy has been developed.
Contrary to the QLattice and Eureqa GPLearn also only uses a single of the input features.
Eureqa here uses the less, max and logistic functions.
Finally we consider the spambase classiﬁcation data set, where the goal is ﬁnd a classiﬁer
that can distinguish between spam and non-spam emails. The QLattice and Eureqa both
use the input features 6 , 51 , 52 , but combine them diﬀerently. Eureqa also here makes
use of the max and min functions. The feature names for this data set as it appears in the
PMLB data set suite does not contain any meaningful names, but looking at the original
source at https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/spambase we see that the feature
with number 6 is the word frequency of remove, number 51 is the character frequency of
exclamation marks and number 52 is the character frequency of dollar signs. The two last
features in particular make very good sense to appear in a classiﬁer on this data set, as we
84
==PAGE:93==
7.4
Algorithm Performance Comparison
7
EVALUATION AND RESULTS
might imagine spam emails with promotions using exclamation marks to emphasise their
oﬀer and dollar signs if the subject is pertinent to saving money or similar.
An interesting general observation we are able to make from this model inspection is that
Eureqa seems to include weights in front of features to a much larger extent than GPLearn.
Moreover, the weights seem to be on vastly diﬀerent scales. This observation suggests that
Eureqa uses a form of coeﬃcient optimisation technique instead of only relying on the evolu-
tionary operators introducing ephemeral scalars chosen from a range uniformly at random.
This aspect is similar to the QLattice tuning weights for each feature using stochastic gradi-
ent descent and seems to be one of the main reasons why Eureqa performs much better than
GPLearn, in particular on the regression data sets. The beneﬁts of explicitly incorporating
weights and tuning them during search has also been documented in the literature to be
eﬀective for genetic programming based symbolic regression [14], which further supports the
conjecture that Eureqa does this as part of the algorithm.

==PAGE:95==
8
DISCUSSION AND FUTURE WORK
8
Discussion and Future Work
We have at this point covered multiple diﬀerent aspects and parameter settings for the QLat-
tice and genetic programming through many benchmarks on diﬀerent types of problems as
well as compared the performance of the QLattice with the state-of-the art genetic pro-
gramming symbolic regression algorithm, Eureqa. Together the tests provide an extensive
and detailed account of the applicability of parameter settings and genetic programming
techniques for the QLattice and how this compares with genetic programming. The theoret-
ical problem benchmarks showed how it is possible to run the QLattice on non-supervised
learning problems, where the goal is to evolve certain structures. As expected the genetic
programming algorithms (1+1) GP and RLS-GP using the HVL-Prime operator performed
much better on the Order and Majority problems, but the QLattice was able to solve the
MAX problem up to a max depth of D = 6 when not using feyn. qgraph . algo x. This
provided some interesting insight into some of the inner workings of the QLattice algorithm,
which was one of the goals with the theoretical tests. The synthetic tests provided test cases
with full knowledge of the structure of the data, which was useful when evaluating how well
the best models found mimicked the underlying formula. We saw how the QLattice was able
to model a sine curve in a limited interval using gaussian interactions and how the genetic
programming algorithms showed a greater tendency to develop bloat. We also observed how
test case 2 involving division proved diﬃcult for the QLattice, which may be explained by
the very high output values when the dividend gets close to 0, which the protected division
operator inverse cannot capture.
The many benchmarks on the PMLB data sets gave
an impression of the performance on real world data sets, where some settings performed
similarly and some settings proved much worse than others. We also observed a general
trend in high test scores on some data sets such as 197 cpu act and low average test scores
on some of the very small data sets like 1096 FacultySalaries and 659 sleuth ex1714,
partly caused by outliers in the test set in a few of the cross-validation folds.
Main Results for the QLattice
The results in Section 7.3 showed among other things
that the default hyper-parameters of the QLattice worked well for both synthetic and real
world data sets. The models were generally also much smaller, where GPLearn and Custom
GP to much larger degree exhibited tendencies to develop large complex tree structures and
a lot of redundancy on some data sets.
The results also highlighted parameter settings diﬀerent from the default of the QLattice
that lead to better predictive performance and simpler models. The Baysian information
criterion (BIC) achieved a slightly higher weighted score than sorting on loss and the Akaike
information criterion (AIC), but achieved a notably higher weighted score on lowest best
model size. The idea of taking model size into account during training and seeing a beneﬁt in
the ﬁnal best model found is also found for genetic programming where parsimony pressure
is used to penalise the ﬁtness of complex models. Interestingly for GPLearn, lowering the
parsimony pressure below the default gave better predictive performance, but made the
best model size much larger. Applying parsimony pressure to the QLattice did not lead
to an increase in performance compared to sorting on loss alone, which suggests that the
information criteria using parameter counts of the functions is a better way forward for the
QLattice. An interesting topic for future work motivated by the good results of BIC would
be to experiment with slight diﬀerences in the parameter counts for the diﬀerent functions
and see if this could increase performance further. It would also be interesting to implement
and sort on another information criterion such as the Hannan–Quinn information criterion
(HQ) [21].
The benchmark of the evolutionary hyper-parameters also gave some interesting results.
Although many combinations of the half life parameters A0, product n · τ, island maximum
distance and mutation strength lead to the same predictive performance, careful inspection
suggests that a mutation strength slightly higher than the default value of 1 is advantageous
87
==PAGE:96==
8
DISCUSSION AND FUTURE WORK
in terms of lowering the model size without compromising predictive performance. Likewise
for increasing the island max distance from 3 and the product n · τ. The results also seem
to conﬁrm that lowering the values below the default as expected had an adverse eﬀect on
model size in particular.
The QLattice and Eureqa
From the experiments in Section 7.4 we saw that the QLattice
performed competitively with Eureqa and thereby achieved state-of-the-art results.
The
QLattice achieved a slightly higher mean test R2 score on the regression test cases and
higher AUC score and Matthews correlation coeﬃcient on the classiﬁcation test cases, while
Eureqa achieved a lower test mean squared error and cross-entropy loss on the regression
and classiﬁcation tests, respectively. Eureqa generalised slightly better in the benchmark,
which was primarily caused by the QLattice achieving a slightly higher diﬀerence between
train and test scores on 207 autoPrice and 643 fri c0 250 10. The diﬀerence in predictive
performance was minimal though and most likely within margin of error. We did not test
statistical signiﬁcance here e.g. by computing conﬁdence intervals of the mean test score
and checking if the intervals for the algorithms overlap or by testing a null hypothesis of
the diﬀerence in mean test score being 0. The reason for this is that only three Monte-
Carlo cross-validation folds were used and a relatively low number of data sets. Further, we
are keeping the results of the regression and classiﬁcation test cases separate meaning we
would have to also have to make adjustments for statistical signiﬁcance to avoid issues with
multiple testing. The end result would likely be the same, namely that there is no signiﬁcant
diﬀerence in predictive performance between the QLattice and Eureqa like we also conclude
from a qualitative judgement. For the same reason we refrained from using statistics tests for
comparing hyper-parameters earlier where we carried out many tests and a major correction
to the certainty would have to be imposed. Instead we have opted throughout this project
for carefully considering the summary results and judging the certainty based on the variance
from visually inspecting plots such as line charts.
The algorithm comparison test mainly focused on predictive performance, but from
the cursory best model inspection, the QLattice and Eureqa were arguably both good at
ﬁnding simple interpretable models.
Eureqa used functions such as max, min, less and
if-then-else which mean the models are not strictly mathematical formulas when using
the default function set and can both be seen as a pro and con in terms of interpretability.
The best models seemed around the same size when taking into account the hidden weight
and bias terms in the QLattice graphs. Eureqa did not seem to have a way to visualise the
best models using the API other than the expression itself, however, where the QLattice can
show a visual representation of the best models where the weights, bias and scale terms are
only shown in a tool tip in an IPython environment like jupyter notebook17. Even though
Eureqa and GPLearn are based genetic programming algorithms, the best models of Eureqa
used weights in front of features to much larger extent like the QLattice does, which is one
of the reasons Eureqa and the QLattice perform better than GPLearn, in particular on the
regression data sets. As mentioned before this may be explained by Eureqa using some kind
of coeﬃcient optimisation step.
Apart from the results for the predictive performance and interpretability, the work
involved in setting up the comparison test also gave some interesting insight into another
important aspect of machine learning modelling, namely the convenience and ﬂexibility of
the API for the algorithms. Datarobot oﬀers a graphical user interface on their website where
the user can upload a data set and the system will automatically perform data imputation,
determine the best performance metric, train multiple diﬀerent types of models and choose
the one that is deemed the best and ﬁnally report an unbiased estimate of the generalisation
error based on a held-out test partition of the uploaded data. The system also includes
a way to deploy a model to production. From a user experience point of view this makes
it easy to get started using the Datarobot machine learning platform. On the other hand
17https://jupyter.org (accessed March 2021)
88
==PAGE:97==
8
DISCUSSION AND FUTURE WORK
it is more challenging to customise this pipeline and although DataRobot oﬀers a Python
API, the workﬂow feels like it is geared towards a more normal type of use. One challenge
for instance was that it was not readily apparent how to extract the train predictions and
train error using the API without having to reupload the train data as a predictions data
set which the system will process and run the model on.
On the website there was a
console window showing output messages about the progress and current best model during
iterations of training, but it was not readily apparent how to extract this information using
the Python API either or how to get the model size without manually parsing the best model
expression. We stress the words readily apparent here as it might be possible to achieve the
things described in an easier way using the Python API, but to the best of our knowledge
based on the time invested in setting up the benchmark, it was not achievable and would
have to be done diﬀerently e.g. using the website dashboard. The QLattice cannot be used
with a no-code graphical interface, but the API oﬀers a lot of control and ﬂexibility, which
made it easy to extract runtime metrics and integrate into a custom analysis pipeline in this
project. Despite the software running the QLattice being hidden and running on a cluster,
much of the training process of the QLattice algorithm happens locally on the client side.
This enabled us in this project to among other things customise the algorithm by using
custom sorting functions and seeing the eﬀect of not using measures such as graph decay in
Section 7.1. The data is also kept local at all time and does not have to be uploaded to a
server in order to train a model or getting a model to make predictions.
One nice feature of Eureqa is that it computes a Pareto front of solutions naturally as
part of the algorithm. This makes it easy for the user of the algorithm to select a trade-oﬀ
between accuracy and model complexity after training has completed. Something similar
can be achieved with the QLattice by a simple custom function that goes through the graphs
in the QGraph head at the end of training and computes the Pareto front. It is important
to note, however, that the Pareto front computed this way does not reliably show the best
possible graph for all complexities, since the search is by default optimising for loss as a single
objective. Had the search been run with a constraint on a certain model complexity, a better
graph might have been found than the one in the Pareto front without the constraint.
Symbolic Regression in General
The QLattice, Eureqa and GPLearn all share many
of the same beneﬁts by virtue of operating in the symbolic regression space. Apart from
documenting a great performance by the QLattice and Eureqa and good results for GPLearn
in some cases, the results of this project also show that there are many beneﬁts of symbolic
regression in general. Most notably that no model structure is assumed beforehand, which
is found as part of the search. This is showcased in particular in the synthetic tests where
we saw how the true underlying formula was found in many cases. The results also show
that with the right set of parameters good performance can be achieved for many diﬀerent
data sets and problems. For the QLattice the default parameters were used for most tests
and lead to good results. For GPLearn the default parameters were used except the full
function set and optionally the predictive performance could be increased at the expense
of a higher running time by increasing the population size. The algorithms also work well
without preprocessing steps as standardisation and one-hot encoding of categorical variables
(assuming they are ordinal encoded), which also makes it easier to work with the algorithms.
We also saw that for both the QLattice, GPLearn and Eureqa the best models generalised
well in many cases and when GPLearn did very poorly in terms of test scores on some
data sets, so did the train scores meaning. The train score is therefore for GPLearn a good
indication of how well the model is performing.
An interesting topic for future work motivated by the good results of the symbolic regres-
sion algorithms in this project, would be to benchmark more symbolic regression algorithms
and see how they compare. This could for instance be recent methods such as AI Feynman
and TuringBot as well as some of the genetic programming algorithms mentioned in [38].
89
==PAGE:98==
8
DISCUSSION AND FUTURE WORK
Custom GP and Visualisation Tool
The implementation of the custom genetic pro-
gramming framework including the (1+1) GP, RLS-GP and SMO-GP made it possible to
run a test for theoretical problems from GP literature and compare with the QLattice. The
Custom GP for regression and classiﬁcation also showed that a genetic programming based
symbolic regression can be implemented without a lot of code and intricacy. The predictive
performance results from the synthetic test cases were competitive with GPLearn and for
some test cases a simpler solution was found. The performance as a function of runtime in
seconds was not on a par with the QLattice and GPLearn, however. Some of the primary
reasons we see a diﬀerence in performance are because the Custom GP uses custom classes
for modelling the tree structure instead of a Polish preﬁx style representation using lists,
which incur less overhead; running the training loop on more than one thread should also
help increase performance. Future work would be to redesign and implement this for Cus-
tom GP, which would likely improve the implementation signiﬁcantly in terms of eﬃciency.
Alternatively another language such as Rust or C/C++ could be used and utilise the struct
construct and the eﬃciency and strong parallelism capabilities that those languages oﬀer to
try to gain a speed up that will make it more competitive.
The visualisation tool has been useful as an aid for exploring the PMLB data sets by
e.g. inspecting the pandas-profiling reports generated for most of the data sets. It
also serves as a prototype for a no-code user interface for running the QLattice and visualise
results during training similar to what Datarobot oﬀers for Eureqa albeit much more limited
in functionality. An interesting topic for future work would be to extend and improve the
functionality and user experience of the visualisation tool. Some ideas for things that could
be improved are as follows: At the moment the frontend does not include any validation of
user input and settings. For instance if the user types in an invalid formula for a synthetic
test case the interface will not alert the user that the test cannot be run. Currently exceptions
thrown by the backend are not shown to the user in the frontend either. The responsiveness
of the user interface could also be optimised. For instance when rendering the best models
on the run page, only small trees for GPLearn and Custom GP are rendered as it requires
too much computation time to render large trees with the current way it is implemented.
Currently the settings speciﬁed on the problem and algorithm pages are reset to their default
values every time the user navigates to them. It would also be more convenient to display the
current settings and have a reset button for resetting them to their default values instead.
Further Analysis
In addition to the ideas already mentioned there are many other ideas
for future work as well. We considered many parameters and aspects in Section 7.3, but other
interesting methods could still be included. One idea would be to use Bayesian optimisation
for exploring the evolutionary hyper-parameter settings in order to try to ﬁnd the best
performing settings in a more sophisticated way than grid-search. It would also be interesting
to explore in more detail the resource usage and eﬀect of the hardware speciﬁcation on the
performance of the algorithms. We could for instance have recorded metrics such as the
number of cache misses, the idle time of each core, the gain and overhead incurred with
increasing the number of threads etc. The results for GPLearn in particular indicated that
the initialisation of the algorithm was important in achieving a high performing model at
the end of training. It would therefore also be interesting to examine in more detail to what
extent this holds for the QLattice as well. One idea worth exploring would be to run the
QLattice with diﬀerent initial random seeds for a few iterations and then continue training
for only the instance that achieved the best validation score. We also saw for GPLearn
that a greater population size gave better predictive performance. It would therefore be
interesting to see if generating more graphs from the QLattice every iteration would be
beneﬁcial as this is the method that most closely corresponds to increasing the population
size in GPLearn as more graphs are considered during training that way and would make
the QGraph head larger.
The work with analysing the QLattice as an evolutionary algorithm in this project has
90
==PAGE:99==
9
CONCLUSION
primarily been on the feyn side of the algorithm. An alternative angle on the project would
be to focus more on the QLattice side of the algorithm with an analysis and investigation of
the techniques and methods applied here. Additionally the work could involve extending the
functionality of the QLattice by for instance experimenting with taking the dimensions of
the features and response in a data set into account. For example if we know that the target
should have the unit km/h, the algorithm could utilise this knowledge by trying to ensure
that the features related to the units kilometres and hour appear as a quotient in the model
expression. Investigation of the hyper-parameters associated with the QLattice component
such as the number of x-locations could be interesting. It could also be interesting to try to
apply the QLattice to a control problem with a feedback loop, where the QLattice models
would be used to change the rules of a dynamic system that aﬀects future input. Control
problems are a problem type where genetic programming has been used and a comparison
between the QLattice and a genetic programming algorithm might therefore also have been
interesting here.
9
Conclusion
In this study we have analysed the QLattice as an evolutionary algorithm and compared
with three genetic programming based symbolic regression algorithms through an exten-
sive analysis and set of benchmarks. We have considered theoretical problems from genetic
programming literature, which showcased how the QLattice can be adapted to run on non-
supervised learning problems. We set up and benchmarked the QLattice on synthetic data
cases, which gave insight into how good the best models generated are. We also performed
an extended bias-variance decomposition like in [39], where it was done for genetic pro-
gramming. We then ran the QLattice on data sets from the PMLB data set suite, where
we tested a wide range of parameter settings and applied evolutionary techniques such as
parsimony pressure, genetic marker density optimisation and novelty search. The metrics
recorded during training suggest that the QLattice is generally robust against overﬁtting
and development of bloat and that diversity in the QGraph head is maintained at a good
level throughout search. Finally, we compared the performance of the QLattice with the
state-of-the-art genetic programming based symbolic regression algorithm, Eureqa. In this
benchmark the QLattice received the best test R2 score and test AUC score, but Eureqa
a better test mean squared error and cross-entropy loss. The diﬀerence in test score be-
tween the two algorithms is small and within margin of error, however, so in conclusion, the
QLattice and Eureqa performed equally well.
The results of the benchmarks show that the QLattice performs well with the default pa-
rameters, which makes it a good oﬀ-the-shelf algorithm. We also found promising alternative
settings of some of the hyper-parameters, however: The Bayesian Information Criterion for
sorting the QGraph head lead to smaller models with equal or better predictive performance
compared to sorting on loss. Furthermore, increasing the mutation strength and the product
n·τ in the equation for the the graph decay threshold also seemed promising in lowering the
model size of the best model while maintaining high predictive performance. Many choices
of parameter settings were also shown to perform worse than the default settings, which
provides guidance for settings that should be avoided and are less relevant in making future
tests for.
The high performance and ease of use of the QLattice and Eureqa are encouraging
for symbolic regression in general as well and the results of the project motivates further
work with the QLattice and symbolic regression algorithms. We have discussed a number
of interesting topics for future work and in particular it would be interesting to continue
exploring hyper-parameter choices of the QLattice using Bayesian optimisation, comparing
more symbolic regression algorithms such as AI Feynman and extending the visualisation
tool developed as part of the project.

Example of computing the genetic marker of four genetic programming trees.
Left: The genetic markers for tree 1, 2 and 3 are computed for depth d = 1
starting at the root level. Tree 1 and 2 have the same genetic marker even
though they diﬀer in the right most leaf node. Right: The genetic marker of
tree 4 for depth d = 1 starting at level 1 in the tree. Because the root has
two subtrees the genetic marker of tree 4 becomes the concatenation of the
genetic markers of the two subtrees. Example from [10]. . . . . . . . . . . . .

Name
Usage
Comments
constant
1.234
Allows solutions to use real-valued constants
integer
7
Allows solutions to use integer constants
add
x + y or add(x,y)
subtract
x - y or sub(x,y)
multiply
x * y or mul(x,y)
divide
x / y or div(x,y)
y must be non-zero
negation
-x
sine
sin(x)
The angle is in radians
cosine

The angle is in radians
tangent
tan(x)
The angle is in radians
exponential
exp(x)
Returns eˆx
logarithm
log(x)
This is the natural logarithm (base e)
factorial
factorial(x) or x!
Computes
the
real
valued
factorial
as
gamma(x+1)
power
x ˆ y or pow(x,y)
x and y could be any expression
sqrt
sqrt(x)
x must be positive
Delayed Variable
delay(x,num rows)
Returns the value of the x variable num rows
prior to the current row
Simple Moving Average
sma(x,num rows)
Returns the average value of the last num rows
values of the x variable
Weighted Moving Average
wma(x,num rows)
Returns
the
arithmetically
weighted
average
value of the last num rows values of the x variable
Modiﬁed Moving Average
mma(x,num rows)
Returns the modiﬁed average value of the last
num rows values of the x variable
Simple Moving Median
smm(x,num rows)
Returns the median value of the last num rows
values of the x variable. If there are an even num-
ber of values, smm returns the ﬁrst value, at the
ﬂoor(length / 2) index.
Logistic Function
logistic(x)
This is a common sigmoid squashing function.
Returns 1/(1+ exp(-x))
Step Function
step(x)
Returns 1 if x is positive, zero otherwise
Sign Function
sgn(x)
Returns -1 if x is negative, +1 if x is positive, and
0 if x is zero
Gaussian Function
gauss(x)
This is a bell-shaped squashing function. Returns
exp(-xˆ2)
Hyperbolic Tangent
tanh(x)
This is a common squashing function. Returns a
value between -1 and +1
Error Function
erf(x)
Integral of the normal distribution.
Returns a
value between -1 and +1
Error Function Complement
erfc(x)
Returns 1.0 - erf(x)
equal to
equal(x,y)
Returns 1 if x equals y numerically, 0 otherwise
less than
less(x,y)
Returns 1 if x < y, 0 otherwise
less than or equal to
less or equal(x,y)
Returns 1 if x ≥y, 0 otherwise
greater than
greater(x,y)
Returns 1 if x > y, 0 otherwise
greater than or equal to
greater or equal(x,y)
Returns 1 if x ≤y, 0 otherwise
if-then-else
if(x,y,z)
Returns y if x is greater than 0, z otherwise
logical and
and(x,y)
Returns 1 if both x and y are greater than 0, 0
otherwise
logical or
or(x,y)
Returns 1 if either x or y are greater than 0, 0
otherwise
logical xor
xor(x,y)
Returns 1 if (x ≥0 and y > 0) or (x > 0 and y
≥0), 0 otherwise
logical not
not(x)
Returns 0 if x is greater than 0, 1 otherwise
Minimum
min(x,y)
Returns the minimum (signed) result of x and y
for the data point
Maximum
max(x,y)
Returns the maximum (signed) result of x and y
for the data point
Modulo
mod(x,y)
Returns the remainder of x/y
Floor
ﬂoor(x)
Returns an integer of x rounded down toward -
inﬁnity
Ceiling
ceil(x)
Returns an integer of x rounded up toward +in-
ﬁnite
Round
round(x)
Returns an integer of x rounded to the nearest
integer
Absolute value
abs(x)
Returns the positive value of x
Arcsine
asin(x)
The inverse of the sine function
Arccosine
acos(x)
The inverse of the cosine function
Arctangent
atan(x)
The inverse of the tangent function
Two-argument
Arctangent
atan2(y,x)
The inverse of the tangent
function
